Dr. Kelly’s reputation is so thoroughly established that it will only oe necessary for him to intimate
that he is practising as a Specialist, to be appreciated as a benefactor to suffering mankind. As the
greater portion of the New South Wales public is aware, Dr. Kelly is a Legally Qualified and Registered
Physician, and has by far the largest practice in the colonies. Many persons at a distance, however are
unaware of his great success, as, hitherto, he has refrained from advertising. Hence, to widen’ the
sphere of his usefulness, he has determined, by means of advertising, to make himself known in remote
districts, where his reputation may not yet have travelled. As the question may arise, why does so
eminent a Physician as Dr. Kelly find it necessary to advertise, he may reply to one and all as
follows That the Physician who has the power to cure, and remains silent, is guilty of an un-
pardonable sin of omission; for if there is a person on earth to be pitied, it is he who is obliged to
associate fellow beings while his mind is constantly harassed and tortured by the consciousness
of his past folly.

A Lodging-House Idyll.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
* Maeriage-TBAPS, every one of them !’ we fancy
we hear some Australian Major Pendennis saying
to his inexperienced nephew; ‘keep out of lodg-
ing-houses at all hazards.’ But the inexperienced
nephew likes to see the world for himself
‘ The first essential to a union between the
sexes,’once said a famous philosopher, ‘is proxi-
mity ; the second is proximity ; the third is proxi-
mity.’ In a lodging-house, these conditions are
secured as perfectly as on board ship. Jn the
lodging-house, there is the constant propinquity
at table with the landlady’s daughters or feminine
lodgers, there is the quiet conversation in the
sitting-room or verandah, the occasional visit to
the theatre in parties of three or four, and the
periodical carpet-dance when mutual friends are
asked to join in the ‘hop,’amid such hilarity as
may be sustained on thick sandwiches and
“ colonial beer. Given, an average young man
and an average young woman, sitting at the same
table for six consecutive months, with outside
flirtations intervening, and the result may bo
safely predicted.
Perhaps it is because the proprietrix knows
this that so many establishments of the kind are
Avillingly carried on at a pecuniary loss. The
lodging-house may therefore be regarded as serving
an important purpose in the social economy of the
community. Despite all that tire cynics may say,
there is no finer school for gaining an intimate
knowledge of human nature. 1 lore the girl of the
period is seen, if not with her night-cap, at
least with her curl-papers on ; and here masculine
selfishness may be observed in all its varying
gradations.
When young Mr. Blusher first came to Sydney
lie Was but a fledgeling. His duties in the Cir-
cumlocution Ollicc were light; his salary was
small, but sufficient to enable him to dress with
that care which he considered duo to society ; and
his appearance was distinctly that of an ‘ eligible.’
It w’as the wish of his denting mamma that he
should bs placed in some respectable family where
he would have matronly supervision and all the
comforts of a home. Looking over the newspaper
advertisements, Mr. Blusher saw" at once that
there would be no difficulty in finding exactly
what was wanted. The trouble was rather on
account of the embarrassing number of oppor-
tunities. _ Nearly every other advt. that met his
eye promised ‘ all the comforts of a home. ’ In a
great many instances was added ‘ latch-key, piano,
baths,’and he wondered greatly what strong re-
commendation could be supposed to lie in that
triple allurement. The announcement which
eventually riA T eted his attention was contained in
the legend ‘no children, and no other boarder.’
Here was, doubtless, a pleasant little family,
which had a larger house than it could fill, and
wished for the society of an agreeable young man.
The happiest couples are known to sigh sometimes
for variety of conversation. A lodger is a peg to
hang an argument on, or a victim at whom to aim
anew old jokes. And there was a delicacy in the
announcement which especially pleased Mr,
Blusher’s fastidious taste. The name of the
advertiser was not given. The applicant for
permission to enter that select abode must
exchange cards at a well-known shop in George-
street, and submit himself to the chance of
refusal. There were not many refusals of young
men, as Mr. Blusher would have guessed had he
been a few years older.
Mr. Blusher left his name with some little fear
and trembling at the place of business indicated,
and by the evening’s post received a pretty little
scented note, dated Amaranthine House, North,
Shore, informing him that Mrs. Hasheru had a
room at his disposal, and asking him to inspect it
at his convenience. It was also added that if the
lady, of the house were not at home when he
called, Miss Hashem would show him the apart-
ment. The. fact was, Mrs. H., who, on seeing
Mr. Blusher’s card, had promptly possessed her-
self of all necessary information concerning him,
was extremely anxious to secure so eligible a
lodger, and the allusion to Miss H. was one of
those little strokes of diplomacy in which she was
an adept,
Mr. Blusher was very favourably impressed,
ihe advt. had said there were no children ; but
there was a young lady—so much the better.
And he liked the name of the residence, ‘Amaran-
thine House. It sounded pleasant and genial; it
suggested a brightly - polished gate - handle, a
canary twittering in its cage on the verandah, and
the sylph-like form of a young girl standing on
tip-toe in a spirituellc attitude.
In due time, Mr. Blusher somewhat nervously
presented himself at Amaranthine House. It was
not a detached cottage or bungalow, as he had
imagined, but one of a terrace of three which
owed its distinctive name to the fertile resource
of its occupants. Nevertheless, it was a sub-
stantial house, and the rooms seemed to be good
enough. A slatternly maid-of-all-work answered
tiro bell and showed him into the front parlour.
Mr. Blusher had shivered slightly at the sight of
the servant-girl. Nothing is more disillusioning
to a young man in search of a picturesque and
cheerful residence than the immediate obtrusion
on his notice of an untidy abigail. How often a
bright and cheery girl at the door prepossesses
one in favour of the inmates ! Besides, the young
man of the period likes a neat-handed ‘ slavey ’
with whom he may interchange a little chaff
when she is dusting his coat or lighting his fire.
Such thoughts rapidly passed through Mr.
Blusher’s mind as he was ushered into the front
parlour. This room was very showily furnished,
though not exactly in the best taste. There was
a glided mirror and a profusion of ornaments on
the chimney-piece with the tail-feathers of a lyre-
bird springing from the centre. The sofa and
chairs were half enveloped in antimaccassars, the
table was loaded with nic-nacs and a flower-vase
supported by a few gaudily-bound books, including
a huge illustrated family Bible. There were two
or three crude crayon drawings on the wall, the
work of some beginner in the art; and large
photographs of the master and mistress of the
house in their bridal costume. Presently there
was a rustic of feminine attire, and Mrs. Hashem
entered. She was a portly, well-preserved lady of
about 42 summers. Her manner was at once
bland and effusive. She naturally concluded that
Mr. Blusher was at the last ball which was the
talk of the town, was sure he was going to the
dinner-party of the Swellbys, and contrived to
soon convey an impression that she and her
daughter were largely in society. Papa, poor
dear, was a commercial man, but he had in-
different health and did not care for gaiety, so he
rarely accompanied them. It would be so pleasant
to have in the house a young gentleman of tun
like Mr. Blusher. -The fact, as Mr. Blusher dis-
covered a few days later, was that papa was a
commercial man with narrow means, who liked to
smoke his pipe at home and who had a profound
contempt for what his wife called ‘ society,’hut
who dared not say so, being kept in complete sub-
jection by his better half.
Mrs. Hashcm babbled on until she was joined
by the daughter, Miss Geraldine. Twice nine
years had shod their blessings on this fair
one’s head. She was attractively dressed in
a light morning robe. A ribbon encircled her
taper waist and fell in festoons at her
side. While culls set off a pair of shapely
hands, and a plain collar with a neat little
brooch at the point of juncture, held in
affectionate embrace a soft, white neck. The
head which was supported by this slender
pillar was noticeable for its brown, curly hair, a
pair of tender, blue eyes which glanced timorously
at the stranger and then retreated behind their
long lashes, a small, heart-shaped mouth, and a
dimpled chin.
Mrs. Hashem presented the young couple to
each other, in a manner that was outwardly
that of deprecative maternal piide. A shrewd
observer, however, might have read beneath
the stern and comprehensive glaneo of the
general who studies his map while weigh-
ing the chances of a campaign. Mr. Blusher
was quite pleased with the artless simplicity
of the young lady. Ho would have been
more charmed had she talked less about the balls,
parties, and theatres, amid which she seemed fo
pass her life. But she was pleasant to look at,
and friendly. Mr Blusher was at an age at
which male youth is especially susceptible to
feminine charms.
He confessed to himself the influence of the
young enchantress. The terms were agreed upon
with the elder of the two, after the daughter had
retired, without cavil. The sight of the hem of
the young girl’s garment disappearing into the
second room, as he emerged from the parlour to
leave by the street door, did suggest to him that
she had been standing in the passage, and listen-
ing at the key-hole to the terms of the financial
arrangement with mamma, but ho dismissed the
idea at once as a wholly unworthy suspicion.
And so Mr. Blusher took up his abode in his
new home, having a bedroom to himself, and the
privilege of dining and sitting with the family. A
more intimate acquaintance with the house
showed him that nearly all the outlay in furniture
had been lavished on the front parlour. The other
rooms were plainly and barely provided. As ho
approached the kitchen there were distinct evi-
dences of squalor and unclcanlinoss. However,
said he to himself, a great deal may be overlooked
for the sake of the pretty girl. In any case, he
was in no humour to be captious. The family
consisted of the lady and daughter who have
already been described ; two sons at day-school,
aged respectively 14 and 10, and a male cousin of
15 who was to stay with them until his schooling
was over. The advertisement had said “no
children but, then, of course, these were young
men.
There was also the husband. He had so little
influence in the house that we had almost for-
gotten to mention him. He was a good-natured
fellow enough when caught by himself; but in
presence of Mrs. H. he was morose and speech-
less. He had no sympathy with her extravagant
social tastes, licr fondness for going to the theatre
with orders, and her tendency to stint the table to
provide the ways and moans for eliowy life in the
parlour. Sad experience had proved to him that
remonstrance was useless ; so he took refuge in a
grim taciturnity. He never by any chance called
his wife by her Christian name. lie had no
appellation for her at all. When he did speak to
her, which was seldom, he generally prefaced his
observations with a ‘ Look here, will you,’as though
she had been a perfect stranger of inferior degree.
Hie circumstance was all the more remarkable
from the face that Mrs. llashein, wnen relating,any
anecdote or pars of the fami y history in which
her husband appeared as addressing her, invariably
expressed herself in this way : “Lucy, my dear,’'
Alfred said to me, ‘ this will never do, you know ,”
&c. Once or twice it flashed across Mr. Blusher's
mind that this was not the best of all trainings
for a young wife to receive. But Geraldine .was
sad, pensive and sprightly by turns ; and “hang
it,, as he observed to himself one evening, “ such a
face and figure would turn Wilderness Row to
Paradise Terrace, and Vinegar Hill to Mount
Pleasant.”
Mr. Blusher’s first dinner at his new place of
residence was very good ; the attention he re-
ceived from the ladies was flattering ; and by the
time he had sab down to a quiet supper in tin;
evening, he was felicitating himself on his happy
circumstances. The meals, however, deteriorated
rapidly after the second or third day. He
observed that Mrs. Ha-hem had a formula which
was just a little irritating. Often, though there
was rarely any soup, after the meat had been
removed, the servant would bring in a fruit pie,
and the lady at the head of the table would; say
rapidly with a comprehensive glance round:
“ 011 will not have any pie ? Very w ell; take it
away, Jane.” In point of fact, no one had had
t ime to say whether ho or she would venture upon
the dish, before it was whisked off the table, with
the object of being coldly furnished forth at uio
parlour supper in the evening to which some
strangers had been invited. Mrs.. 11. was fond of
introducing the dinner with the remark, in
theatrical parlance, that they w ould begin on the
p-ece de resistance. On one of these occasions- Mr.
Blusher w r as provoked into the tart observation
that he hoped the after-piece would not be a farce.
His untimely wit was received with a scowl by
the landlady, and he saw for a moment a stony
look in the fair Geraldine’s face, which he thought
made her positively plain. Those little incidents,
however, were but as the passing clouds which
temporarily obscure the most beautifu landscape.
To playing duets with the young lady of the
house Mr. Blusher had no objection, but he could
have wished that her mamma would ask him less
often for orders for the theatre. It is a dismal
piece of business that of visiting a large theatre on
a “papery” night. The scanty audience, the
long rows of ladies in the dress-circle with the
proportion of about one gentleman to twelve, and
the pervading smell of cleaned gloves, all proclaim
that it is one of those occasions when the dead*
heads are in full force. Even the actors seem t >
feel the dispiriting influence; the play drags ;
there is a melancholy draughtiness about the
house ; the applause sounds hollow and unnatural;
and there is no hearty enjoyment in the entertain-
ment. Mr. Blusher would have boon less com-
plaisant in this matter than he was, only when ho
first demurred to performing this duty Geraldine
said, in a very natural tone of voice: “Never

•orders. 1 The Hashems wore acquainted with
Mr. Jones, who once lived in the house. Mr.
Jones was the last person in the world to trouble
himself about anything on their behalf, but Mr.
Blusher did not know that, and the ruse always
took. Whenever he showed signs of remissness,
t his holding of Jones in terror cm always brought
him to book.
In course of time Mr. Blusher also found it
somewhat of a boro to have to make himself
agreeable to all the visitors of his friends, and to
be .obliged against his will to assist Miss Geraldine
in her infantile flirtations with some of them. On
these occasions she was studiously distant to him,
but the entente cordiale was generally resumed
next day, though sometimes with signs of linger-
ing irritation on Mr. Blusher’s part. The playing
of the “Blue Danube ’ waltz, arranged as
a duct in such a way that the hands of the
two performers must cross, and necessarily
touch in crossing, would effectually remove
the smart. It was on the occasion of one
of these little musical exercises that Mr.
Blusher was betrayed into an indiscretion which
he afterwards regretted. He had taken more
than his usual quantity of wine that day, so, in-
spirited by the alcohol, and further encouraged by
a slight pressure from the hand of Miss Geraldine,
he suddenly lowered his head and imprinted a
crushing kiss on her ripe lips. Had, he fired a
pistol in her car, ho could not have been more
startled at the result. The young lady jumped
from her seat as though she had been shot, ran to
a neighbouring ottoman, and, burying her face in
her hands, wept as though her heart would break,
Mr. Blusher’s awkward attempts at apology were
indignantly repelled. Never, she exclaimed, as
soon as her sobs would allow her to speak, had
she been so insulted before. Pardon was out of
the question, and if papa only knew, it would be
impossible for Mr. Blusher to remain another hour
in the place. The young man was truly grieved,
and begged forgiveness in a most pathetic way.
At last Miss Geraldine dried her tears, and pro-
mised to say nothing about the matter, but, at the
same time, she felt constrained to tell him that ho
had wrecked her happiness, and that she could
never be the same girl again.
It was early in the evening when this untoward
event occurred. It had made Mr. Blusher feel
truly miserable. In the first place, he had never
thought that a friendly attention of the kind would
have occasioned the young lady distress; and, in
the second place, she had created a sort of halting
feeling in his mind that he had committed himself
in a manner which left him only one honourable
way out of the difficulty—that of making a formal
offer of marriage. When Mr. Blusher entered
that house, nothing was further from his thoughts
than a conjugal union with anyone. Ho was
but 23 years of age ; his income from the cireum-
locution office was only .£l5O per annum, and he
nad no expectations. Apart from having no desire
to “ settle ” just now, marriage with him, for
commercial reasons, was quite out of the question.
But he appeared to have deeply wounded the
girl’s feelings, and the thought of that occasioned
dim genuine concern. When he left the house for
El stroll to think the matter quietly over by him-
self, he was in no enviable state of mind. He was
sormented with the thought of the unhappy girl
jobbing on her mamma’s bosom, or weeping
silently and alone in the darkened recess of her
3wn room.
There is nothing like a “weed” to soothe the
agitation of perplexing thoughts. Mr. Blusher
had forgotten his cigar-case. He would return to
Amaranthine House, steal through the passage
unobserved upstairs to his room, possess himself
of the useful allayer of nervous perturbation, and
resume his promenade within the sound of the
gently lapping waves. Mr, Blusher was at the
house door and about to enter with his latch-key
when the sounds of light laughter directed his
attention to the front parlori' - . Ho could not
refrain from looking through the Venetian blinds
of the window. A friend of the family had arrived
with two midshipmen from a merchant vessel
lying at Circular Quay. Geraldine was sitting
down at table with them, and a x’ound game at
cards had just begun. No one who had soon
her live minutes before in the depths of well-
simulated grief and injured modesty would have
believed it was the same girl, so great was the
transformation. She was radiant with smiles, the
peal of merrv laughter which attracted Mr.
Blusher’s attention to the window had just
ascended from her lips, and she was evidently in
the brightest mood for enjoyment. There was no
trace of pain upon her face—all was happiness,
Mr. Blusher was petrified. His first feeling was
that of surprise, not altogether untinged with
annoyance. Then ho heaved a sigh of relief, as
though a load had boon taken off his mind. “ I
was mistaken,” ho said to himself. “I have
caused no pain ; I have inflicted no injury. I have
incurred no moral obligation—thank God ! I am
free ! ” The young man possessed himself of bis
cigars, and returned to the shore a changed being,
His‘bosom’s lord sat lightly on its throne; Ids
step was light; his carriage was erect and easy ;
and young Sparks, a casual acquaintance whom
he met enjoying the evening sea-breeze, after-
wards remarked that ho never know Blusher to
be so sunny-natured and entertaining,
Blusher did not meet Geraldine again till the
following day, when she was pensive and shy, and
looked at him reproachfully with a saddened air.
There was an additional alertness in the demean-
our of Mrs. Hashcm, and a look of mild triumph
might bo detected lurking in her eye. In (ho
evening the young man and the maiden were
alone together in the parlour. Mr. Blusher was
at his ease. “It is diamond cut diamond, now,”
ho said to himself. Geraldine looked troubled,
and had the appearance of one suffering inwardly,
At length she began, in a voice slightly tremulous
with emotion :
“Of course, Mr. Blusher, there is a great dis-
parity in our positions, and papa is a poor man,
you know ; but I have been speaking w ith mamma,
and she has really taken quite a liking to you, so
that I think if—that is—l can count on over-
coming any difficulty you may anticipate on the
part of my family.”
“ I am afraid there is some misapprehension,”
responded Mr. Blusher - . “I do not contemplate
any ne w arrangement. My agreement with Mrs.
Hashcm has been carried out satisfactorily so far,
I believe.”
. “Then you mean to say,” observed Geraldine,
reddening, “ that your attentions to me last night
were not intended to be accepted in the light of a
proposal.”
“Nothing was further from my thoughts,”
calmly replied Mr. Blusher. “I have already
expressed my regret for having been betrayed into
an act of ungentlemanly presumption, and I am
quite willing to apologise again ; but I pretest that
you have no j ight t o attribute to me motives
which Ido not entertain. Let ns be friends as
before, and all will be well.”
“ But all will not bo well,” suddenly chimed in
a third voice. It was that of Mrs. Hasheni, who
had heard every word of the dialogue from behind
a flowered screen in one corner of the room. ‘‘ I
will have you to understand,” continued the now
irate lady, “ that you cannot trifle with my
daughter’s feelings with impunity. If you do not
do what is right by her, thcits are the law courts
to appeal to. The Minister at the head of your
department is also an old friend of mhif ”
Mr. Blusher was taken aback. This sudden
opening of a masked battery oh him almost bereft
him of his senses. He begged for time to consider
his position, and respectfully assured Mrs. Hashem
that he was incapable of doing anything that he
could not justify.
“ Then you justify making love to an innocent
young girl in the absence of her natural protector,”
said Mrs. Hashem, in a high recitative, “ and
taking a gross liberty with her. lam sure Mr.
Hashem never dared to think of kissing mo till
after wc were married. ’ ’
Mr. Blusher thought that if the lady had re-
ceived no such attentions until after marriage,
her knowledge of them must be small, but ho did
not say so. Deprecating the use of harsh words,
he called for a truce, promising that ho would
resume the conversation with Mrs. Hashem next
day, when they would all be in a calmer frame of
mind. In truth, the position was not only i:rita-
ting, but serious. The soft-headed British jury-
man is proverbially soft-hearted when a pretty
girl is the plaintiff, and Mr. Blusher already had
visions of the local .Serjeant Buzfuz thundering
against him in presence of twelve good men and
true. Quito undecided in his own mind as to
what ho would do, he determined on the following
morning to consult Mr. Jones, with whom he was
slightly acquainted, and who, as wc have already
seen, had once lived with the family.
Jones was a bluff, hearty man of the world, and
took in. the whole situation at once. “ Oh, you’re
with the Hashems, are you?” said he. “ Trying
to hood yo, eh? They had a shot at me, and Tom
Jilter as well. I admired their enterprise, but
hang me if I can understand what they want to
meddle with you for. You haven’t a cent in the
world, have you ?”
Mr. Blusher admitted that when his weekly
washing-bill was paid he would not have so much
as that small coin left.
“ I have it,” suddenly shouted Jones. “ This is
glorious! You are Arthur Blusher, and they
mistake you for cousin Algernon Blusher, who, as
you know, is the adopted heir of LuUockybong.
This comes of putting only your initial on your
card instead of tho Christian name in full. I will
see Mother Trapem and set everything right for
you.”
Jones was as good as his word. He called on
Mrs. Hashem. What passed need not be related.
Suffice ic to say that Mr. Blusher was never again
taken to task in regard to his attentions to Miss
Geraldine, and that both that lady and her
mamma treated him ever afterwards with studied
coldness, not to say indifference. But he pined
under these altered conditions. He could no
longer reconcile himself to a house the joy of
which had flown. He made up his mind to move
a few days after the occurrence of this disillu-
sionary episode, and he determined that his next
experiment in the house of the stranger should bo
where there were other lodgers to keep him in
countenance.

Mr. Macartney, of Waverley Station,
Broadsound (Q.), lias just received information
that a man on Auvergne Station, Victoria R,,
Gulf of Carpentaria, in which lie is interested’
has been killed by the blacks. This will be no
new sensation to Mr. Macartney, who has been on
Waverley since about 1861, when the station was
first stocked, and when it was just about as
“bad ” with blacks as any run in Australia has
ever been. Waverley extends from the Broad-
sound Range to the sea, and consists largely of
marine plains, intersected by salt-water creeks
with pipe-elay banks. The blacks used to play
the very mischief there in the early sixties, and
when the native troopers went after them,
generally escaped by swimming these creeks. The
pipeclay was so boggy that the troopers’
horses could only follow into the water with
difficulty, and the detachment used to return to the
head station snow-white—horses, equipments,
blackfellows and all. One of Macartney’s stock-
men once came on a nigger in the middle of one
of the plains, and hunted him with his stockwhip
going till the poor devil managed to plunge into a
reedy swamp with his carcase seamed with cuts
that a sabre might have made. This is the same
Macartney who was once found by a visiting
squatter docking the tails of a lot of colts, and,
being asked what he was about, replied “Making
five-pound notes.” Docking does give a ribbed-
home, cobby look to even a lanky sort of weed. By
the way, Macartney’s father is the venerable Dean
of Melbourne, now 93 years of age, a son of Sit
John Macartney, Bart., who w r as a member of the
last Irish House of Commons, and, to his credit,
an unflinching opponent of the Act of Union when
money bribes and titles were being showered on
any member who would vote for the Union.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with u'hom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Arts'
Italian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 toords, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tub Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS.
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But tve desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 22, 1892.
Shiels & Co., Bankers, &c.
So far, despite the pressure brought to bear
upon it by the Victorian people, the Shiels
Government has successfully dodged the respon-
sibility of bringing the accused land-boomers of
Melbourne to trial. The “Davies Companies”
—that half-dozen or so of disreputable bubbles
which lived on the reputation of the ex-Speaker,
the Land Credit Bank, the Real Estate ditto,
and the infamous Mercantile are every day
supplying fresh revelations, and each revela-
tion is more unsavoury than the last. Most of
them were built partly on falsehood and partly
on bushranging and plain, unvarnished pocket-
picking ; among them they included nearly
every variety of gentlemanly fraud which a
respectable man of business could perpetrate
with a reasonable chance of keeping out of
gaol ; and, taken all together, they have made
the name of Australia a bye-word and a dis-
grace. There is, in the whole crowd, hardly a
single redeeming feature. In all Australia
there is not one widow or orphan, one dotard
or cripple, or old, blind woman, whose savings
these companies neglected to rake in con-
scientiously when the opportunity presented
itself. Their claws reached out in all direc-
tions to grab from infants, idiots and Chinamen,
from old men and dying consumptives, to scoop
in the scanty savings of servant girls and toil-
worn labourers, and to misappropriate the sub-
stance of everybody who could be deluded by
garbled accounts and lying balance - sheets.
And now that the stupendous swindle is exposed
the principal anxiety of the State is apparently
to protect the authors of all this misery from
criticism and to see that no offensive share-
holder comes between the wind and their
nobility.
* * »
It is the fashion among the Victorian press to
regard the Shiels Cabinet as a weak, boneless,
spineless organisation which all parties can
kick with impunity. Yet in reality it would
seem that the present Government is one of the
strongest which has held office in Victoria for
many years. It stands straight up with a load
of disgrace upon its head which would have
long since crushed a weak Administration to
the dust. It habitually shakes hands with a
dozen more or less discredited bank directors,
and still doesn’t die. It refuses to put on their
trial a crowd of financiers whose glaring mis-
deeds have been proclaimed in every newspaper
in the land, and who have never dared to vin-
dicate their characters by prosecuting even one
of their traducers : it openly blocks the course
of j hs i ice despite about the strongest prhtm
facie evidence that was ever adduced against
any assemblage of speculators ; and it survives
all this. The position it occupies looks very
like that of the man who “buttons” for a
three-card sharper, or a “ chucker-out ” in a
gambling hell. A weak Government would
long since have thrown the boomers to the
wolves and so got rid of a pressing danger, but
only a strong Government—or an exceedingly
reckless one—could afford to associate with
these individuals, and thereby run the risk
of being mistaken for their accomplice. For
when a Cabinet goes out of its way to pro-
tect those whom all the nation execrates—and
apparently execrates with good cause—the
public is certain to suspect either that it does
so from corrupt reasons—in other words, that it
has been bought with hard cash or its equiva-
lent; or else that it is itself involved in the
great land-boom swindle and dare not move for
fear of exposure. The Cabinet which doesn’t
die of such suspicions as these may be suffi-
ciently unprincipled, but at all events it is not
so weak as it looks.
* * *
The Shiels Ministry makes no attempt to
disguise its position which is, briefly, that it
will have no hand in bringing any wealthy
suspect to justice if such a thing can possibly
be avoided. A few days ago it was called upon
by a ruined shareholder to institute proceedings
against the directors of an institution which
recently broke up under peculiarly suspicious
circumstances. The case, as abundantly proved
during the liquidation proceedings, was that the
balance-sheets were a glaring lie, that the
alleged profits were non-existent, that the
dividends were not earned but paid out of
deposits, and that at the time when the
management falsely represented the company
to be prosperous, and congratulated the share-
holders on its success, it was utterly insolvent.
As the directors had signed the whole series of
fabricated balance-sheets it was demanded that
they should be put on trial upon the evidence
of their own autographs and their own speeches.
And in reply Premier Shiels announced that
he would not move in the matter unless legal
evidence was forthcoming that the managers
had published the balance-sheet complained of,
that the said document was false in some dis-
tinct particular, and that they were aware of
its falsity. The brutal cynicism of that reply
hardly requires comment. Either the manage-
ment published the balance-sheet, or else it
published itself, but as the management did not
repudiate it afterwards it may be fairly assumed
that the amazing document did not publish
itself. It was obviously false inasmuch as it
declared the company to have abundant assets,
and the company straightway burst with prac-
tically no assets at all. And as the managers
of the institution had attested the false balance-
sheet and thereby made themselves responsible
for its contents, it was for them to disprove the
testimony which they had already furnished in
abundance against themselves.
• # w
The demand for “ legal evidence ” in such a
case was, as Shiels or any other lawyer must
be well aware, asking the impossible. Legal
evidence can only be adduced against any
person after his arrest by means of sworn wit-
nesses, and the announcement that such evi-
dence must be forthcoming before any arrest is
made is equivalent to saying that the accused
must be tried and found guilty before the case
begins. The State never travels on this doc-
trine when any common, uninfluential “sus-
pect ” is concerned. When a murder or a
burglary is committed it seizes somebody
on a reasonable suspicion and finds the
legal evidence afterwards to the best of
its ability. If it declined to arrest the
homicide until the “ legal evidence ” was
forthcoming, then the after trial would be
altogether unnecessary; he would be proved
guilty first, then he would be captured by the
police, and after that he would be hanged as
soon as convenient. Under such conditions
nobody ever would be hanged. Premier Shiels’
dictum, as regards the bank - wreckers of
Victoria, may be condensed into a sentence:
They must be convicted by absolute legal
evidence befoi’e the Law Department will order
their arrest; they can’t by any possible process
be convicted until after their arrest unless the
State gives the shareholders power to empanel
a jury and take evidence on oath, and act as a
court of law generally ; therefore, the State is
waiting for the impossible, and when the impos-
sible comes about it will do its duty in the
matter without fear or favour. To all intents
and purposes the Cabinet might as well
announce that it will take proceedings as soon
as perdition becomes cold and Time begins to
walk backwards.
• • *
The Shiels Government apparently rather
enjoys the position than otherwise, and takes a
certain pleasure in setting decency at defiance.
It still includes among its leading members one
of the directors of the defunct Mercantile Bank
—an institution which seems to call for as much
enquiry as any of the other land-boom com-
panies ; the same director who delivered a
master-piece of artistic misrepresentation to the
shareholders of that unhappy company just when
its condition was hopeless, and who was after-
wards compelled to retire from his position as
liquidator, apparently because of a unanimous
opinion that he was unfit for the post. There
is no reason to believe that there was any trace
or semblance of actual dishonesty in Graham
Berry’s dealings with the Mercantile Bank,
but still he was a member of a particularly
shady directorate, and now he is a member of
the Cabinet which whitewashes him and his
former colleagues, and is one of the jury which
acquits them and himself without a trial.
Another ex-land boomer, an avowed friend of
Matthew Davies and the same man who roars
himself hoarse in defence of that fallen personage
at public meetings, occupies the Speaker’s chair,
and attempts to smother Parliamentary criticism
on his friends and their doings. Apparently
the Shiels Cabinet approves of this fatted
autocrat and his methods ; at all events it takes
no steps to shift him from his position, or to
prevent him whirling his official robes and wig
in the polluted atmosphere as an advertisement
for the Davies elan. A fallen and execrated
banker, against whom charges of crookedness
are thick, is Agent-General for the province ; he
has been invited, in soft, low tones, to return
to Victoria and explain matters, but he hasn’t
come, and, so far, there are no definite signs of
his coming, and apparently the Shiels Cabinet
has no desire to put any pressure upon him. A
Government, like an individual, is known by
its friends. Bent is the friend of the Shiels
Cabinet, and Matthew Davies is the friend of
Bent, and the Shiels Administration is the
friend of Davies at second hand, and the friend
of Munro and Berry at first hand, and the
bank-boomers form a happy family.
* # #
The resolution—recently announced by cable-
gram —of the London shareholders to prosecute
the directors of the Mercantile Bank was prac-
tically a foregone conclusion. The Shiels
Government could not possibly save its friends
from prosecution, and, as a gathering of pre-
sumably sane men, it was doubtless aware of
the fact. What it craved, apparently, was
only the gratuitous disgrace of having blocked
the course of justice as long as it could, and
of having no personal share in placing any well-
connected personage on his trial. Whether
the accused boomers are convicted or
acquitted, the result would have been the same
if the State had instituted the proceedings
instead of leaving the work to private hands,
therefore the Cabinet gets nothing but the
obloquy, and it gets that in abundance. It
has chosen to be a maityr in the sacred cause
of the fraudulent bank and the disgraced land-
boomer, and when it is buried at next general
election, its epitaph will read that it died in
defence of the bloated man in the big white
waistcoat.
Theology in State Schools.
A letter recently written to the press by New
South Wales ex-Minister of Education Car-
ruthers raises the old question as to the
teaching of religion in the State Schools. The
clergy are not content that the State shall
provide the secular education which all recog-
nise as necessary for the proper performance of
the duties of life; they insist upon the teaching
in State Schools of religion and theology. They
are not satisfied that the State funds shall be
made use of to give children a fair start in life’s
battle ; they seek to divert the State revenue
to the promotion and inculcation of certain
special doctrines and superstitions. The R.O.
Church is only consistent with her own prin-
ciples in making the demand for public money
to be given to her schools to be devoted to the
maintenance and extension of the cause of the
Church. Such a demand, however, must be
resisted, because it would be an extension of
the pernicious principle of the State endowment
of theology in New South Wales. We say, an
extension of this principle, because we contend
that there is, in New South Wales, a practical
endowment of orthodox Protestant theology.
While this is so, the Roman Catholics have a
certain amount of justification in the complaints
that they make and a logical basis for their
most extravagant demands. But there is much
greater justification for the complaints of those
who do not believe at all in the State endow-
ment of religion and theology. The plain fact
is that the Baptists, Congregationalists, Wes-
leyans, Presbyterians, and others, while pro-
fessedly opposed to a State Church and to the
special endowment of a particular form of
faith, give their countenance and support to a
system of State education whereby theology is
virtually established as a State institution,
whereby the public-school teachers are really
State officials empowered to instruct the chil-
dren in certain features of popular belief, and
whereby the clergy of the various churches, or
their subordinates, are allowed to enter the
school buildings, upset the order of secular
studies, and promulgate those doctrines and
dogmas which are characteristic of orthodox
Protestant Christianity. We repeat, there-
fore, that there is a State endowment of ortho-
dox theology in New South Wales. The power
of priestcraft has got a firm hold upon that
system by which the children are to be educated
and fitted for the proper performance of life’s
duties.
# • •
The New South Wales State educational
system has, undoubtedly, many most admirable
features ; but in some respects it is anomalous
and absurd. It is a compromise between the
modern spirit of true liberty and the old spirit
of clerical assumption and priestly intolerance.
There is not even one uniform set of class-books ;
some of the schools, more particularly nominated
by the clerical element, prominently use a book
in which are taught the doctrines of the god-
head of Christ, his substitutionary sacrifice
upon the cross, the necessity for salvation
through his death, the final destruction of the
universe, the day of judgment, and the eternal
hell. This is part and parcel of the ordinary
school work ; besides which, special facilities
are given to the Protestant priesthood to enter
the school-buildings and give instruction that
may be more definite still. In view of all this,
can it be denied that there is a State endow-
ment of theology in New South Wales ?
* * «
Priestcraft has got a- hard grip upon the
public schools ; and the system of education has
to suffer in consequence. And yet this system
is supported by many among the various de-
nominations which profess not to believe in the
State endowment of religion at all. We can
respect the Roman Catholics for being logical
and consistent in their demands, even while we
strenuously resist the granting of those
demands ; but we cannot feel much respect for
those who pretend to be opposed to the con-
nection of Church and State, who profess to be
antagonistic to the national endowment of any
theological system, and yet are the supporters
and advocates of a system of education which
creates an established form of theology in the
province, which makes the State-school teacher
an official representative of orthodox Chris-
tianity, and which devotes the national revenue
to the promulgation of a definite creed.
# * *
Surely the day cannot be very far distant
when New South Wales will refuse any longer
to subsidise any dogma or superstition, when
ilh) Stale-officials in the schools will leach the
ordinary subjects of a secular education, and
when the revenue will not be devoted to sup-
porting the arrogant demands of a priesthood,
either Romish or Protestant. Towards this
result, all those should work who believe in the
absolute separation of Church and State, and
in the principle that the State should not
undertake to teach religion, its policy being to
let the fittest creed survive.
* * #
True, the opposite principle has been power-
ful in the past. It was thought to be the duty
of the State to teach religion to the people, to
provide for the salvation of their souls, and
even to see that they attended church regu-
larly. That was the principle that ruled all
through the Dark Ages, That was the
principle which founded the Inquisition,
invented the thumb-screw and the rack, and
lit the martyr-fires in many lands. That
was the principle which led to many a fierce
and desperate war, which drenched the soil of
Europe, Asia and America with the blood of its
victims, and which has promoted all kinds of
religious enmity and bitterest persecution.
That is the principle that maintains the Estab-
lished Churches in England and Scotland. That
is the principle that converts the New South
Wales public schools into theological seminaries,
and devotes the revenue of the country to sup-
porting the demands of the Protestant clergy.
The logical alternative is to carry out the prin-
ciple of the entire separation of Church and
State, and to decide' that theology shall no
longer be taught by State officials or be sup-
ported by State funds.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Bent Again.
Thomas Bent, Speaker of the Victorian Assem-
bly, has been showing his hand more than ever
at a civic luncheon. The fury of Bent was on
this occasion directed against all newspapers,
but especially the Argus, which “try to dis-
honour some of the leading men in Melbourne
and to run down institutions with which they
are connected.” Such conduct, in the opinion
of Bent, is “ disloyal,” “ disgraceful,” and
entirely disgusting. The Argus, he said—and
with a good deal of truth—did its level best to
push the land-boom along in the piping times
that are no more, and yet “ it was the first to
cry stinking fish.” “It had lauded and magni-
fied the very men ” —here Bent became biblical
—“of whom it now said ‘Crucify them!
Crucify them !’ ” And because the Tory organ
couldn’t, or wouldn’t, see in the first instance
that the land-boom was an evil thing, and
didn’t know that its authors ought to have been
crucified for the sake of their country several
years ago, it has no business to be ordinarily
honest or rational ever again ! Unless Bent
wishes to insinuate that the Argus knew all
along of the financial jugglery and reckless
fiddling with public money that has recently
come to light, there is no sense in his
howl. As matters stand, the daily journal he
despises would seem to have a better sense of
decency than Tom Bent, and that’s all about it.
Bent and the Argus were both friends of
“ certain public men ” before their banks went
bung and their characters were damaged, but
whereas the latter chooses to turn its back on
damaged characters, the former denies the
possibility of any leading man being a
“ damned bad lot ” and deserving of a term in
gaol. The impudence of Bent’s bluff is as dis-
tressing as the fact that it can pass muster for
“manly uprightness” at a Mayor of Mel-
bourne’s luncheon. A Speaker who protests
against the “running down” of shamefully
busted banks, and would choke the cry of
stinking fish merely because the fish, being big,
stink the awfuller, is worthy to be held up as a
typical calamity in Victorian public life. In-
deed he holds himself up as such, and when the
Argus says “God help Victoria if the com-
mercial morality of Mr. Speaker—as an ardent
advocate of hushing up —were the commercial
morality of the colony,” The Bulletin says
“ Amen.” Yea, even “Amen” to the Argus,
for once in a way.
*. —-
Crown Evidence and Murderers.
Hardly a ghastlier horror can be imagined
than that which comes from Brisbane, Q., in
connection with the double hanging of Monday.
George Gleeson was recently convicted at
Cooktown, N.Q., of having murdered, in the
lonely solitudes of Prince of Wales Island, his
mate, Patrick M'Kiernan. When sentenced
to death Gleeson joked with the Judge, laugh-
ingly pointing out that his case—in fact, his
carcase—was about to be taken to a “ higher
court,” and asking his Honor’s opinion as to what
sort of an angel he would make. A Portuguese
named Moncado came next on the list, charged
with having abominably mutilated and mur-
dered a Port Darwin boy. Mr. Gleeson, the
prospective angel, was placed in the dock as a
witness ! He swore that, while fraternising in
the prison cell with Moncado, the latter had
confessed to having perpetrated the several
barbarities of which he stood accused. Mon-
cado confessed to Gleeson ! And this is
evidence ! And Gleeson had already been sen-
tenced to death and was, in the eye of the law, a
dead man ! The evidence of a legal corpse—an
angel!—was relied upon by “the Crown ” to con-
vict another alleged murderer ! But this is not
nearly all the horror of this narrative. Last week,
“ under the sweet influences of religion,” that
angel confessed to a clergyman that what he had
sworn in Court about Moncado was all false.
Gleeson had only wanted another Court scene,
in order to air his wit before the Judge, this
time from the witness-box instead of from the
dock. Grotesquerie of tragedy! He only
wanted a mate on the gallows ! Of course, it
is equally probable that what Gleeson said
last is untrue, as it is that what he said in the
witness-box was untrue. He may have been only
once more acting witli a view to attract the
special regard of spectators. He is probably a
crank. It takes a crank to joke with the Judge
who condemns him, though lots of people have
been known to jest familiarly with their last
valet, the executioner. But The Bulletin
holds (Imt “ ill*' Grown " should not have asked


lor, nor accepted the evidence of, its own con-
victed and condemned (and legally dead) mur-
derer. It holds that Moncado’s conviction
was utterly invalid. “ The Crown ” searches
high and low for evidence against an ac-
cused, but is stone-deaf to any special
appeals on behalf of anyone, unless that party
be “influential” and “respectable.” “The
Crown ” will accept the testimony of any per-
juring detective, any make-a-case policeman,
any pimp, or tout, or hireling— any ruffian at
all—and, as it is now seen, of a convicted
murderer— against an accused ; but it will be
most incredulous and critical concerning “ the
evidence of doubtful characters,” as the writer
once heard an Australian judge protest, if that
evidence should happen to be in favour of the
prisoner. A thoughtful observer, whose oppor-
tunities for noting such matters have been
sufficiently numerous to give his opinions on
this matter weight, asks—“ Is * the Crown ’ a
high school of perjury ?” Experience affirms—
whatever you please. But “the Crown” hanged
Moncad© along with Gleeson all right, last
Monday,
What is a Protectionist Tariff?
Sydney Evening News, which has for the past
five years been making believe that it favours
Freetrade in preference to Protection, showed
only last week that it still gropes in a dismal
intellectual fog. It expressed amazement that
a Protectionist politician should have proposed,
and a Protectionist Ministry endorsed, an
abstract resolution in favour of a progressive
land-tax. The News apparently has innocently
accepted the present New South Wales revenue
tariff as a Protective tariff, and has not yet
succeeded in realising the fundamental fact
that just in so far as a tariff yields revenue it
fails to give Protection, and that essentially
there is no earthly reason why a Protectionist
should not be a Single-taxer, if he feels
that inclination. The ideal of Protection-
ist legislation excludes a Customs tariff
altogether. The object is to exclude from
a community all commodities which can
l>e advantageously produced by its own mem-
bers, not to fine people for importing, but
to prevent them from importing such articles at
all. Scientific Protection does not tax. And
inasmuch as a protective tariff is successful just
in the degree that it fails to provide revenue,
and as the State cannot be carried on without
an income, the Protectionist looks every-
where except to the Custom House for revenue.
It is really he and not the Freetrader, who
detests Customs revenue. It is really he
and not the Freetrader who naturally directs
Ins attention to superfluous wealth, property,
land, for the necessary funds for the mainten-
ance of the public service. The real protec-
tionists in the New South Wales Legislature
are to-day storming at the Ministry because the
tariff yields £BOO,OOO per annum. Mr. Burns,
a downright Freetrader, when Treasurer in
Parkes’ original freetrade administration, had
no objection toCustoms revenue. With a curious
obliquity of mind he, objected to any Customs
taxation which did not yield revenue, and
consequently did tend to encourage local
production. As a matter of principle the
present tariff might be continued in New South
Wales, with trifling modification, by a Freetrade
administration, without any abandonment of
principle. And without great modification in a
contrary direction it cannot be continued by
the Protectionist Government except by a very
serious sacrifice of principle, The only real
reason why a Freetrade Government could not
continue the existing New South Wales tariff,
slightly altered, is because Freetraders and
Freetrade organs and members of Parliament
have either stupidly or maliciously agreed or
pretended to agree to regard it as a real sample
of Protectionist fiscalism.

The Uncertainty of Humour.
Melbourne 'Erald, commenting on the addi-
tion of four “ working-men ” to the Maoriland
Legislative Council, observes that “ the spec-
tacle of, say, a working carpenter entitled to be
addressed as the ‘Hon. John Smith ’ is not
without a certain amount of humour.” A news-
paper edited by a parson isn’t expected to be
much of an authority on humour, but as an
exponent of the gospel of snobbery in simple
faith it would naturally carry weight. To
canting respectability, then, there is a flavour
of ridicule about the term “Honourable” when
applied to any mechanic on active service. The
title doesn’t harmonise with his common felt
hat, or inferior tweed coat, and is incompatible
with the state of earning one’s living in soiled
shirt-sleeves. The Fitchetts of this world may
not deny, once and for all, the possibility of
creatures like carpenters being honourable in
the dictionary meaning of the term, but
they can scarcely associate it, in an official
sense, with any “ unrepresentative ” per-
son. And no manual labourer can be
representative, because physical toil is a
thing apart, so to speak, from official life.
This is the doctrine of land-boomers, clergy,
stock-brokers, civil-servants, and non-producing
citizens in general. Carpenters and such like
have been known to assert that the average
knighthood is a ghastly joke, and to declare
that they see a certain amount of 1 umour in
Fitchett’s reverent reference to h s patron,
“Sir” Matthew Davies, but the question as
to what really constitutes humour is difficult to
decide. Some people laugh bitterly at Tom
Bent’s occupation of the Speaker’s chair, others
shake their sides at Sir Bingy Bingy’s schedule,
many take a comic view of the ’Erald’s tender
regard for broken bank directors, but whether
any of these matters are as funny as the John
Smith idea is a problem too abstruse for casual
discussion.
Women and Votes.
Bear Bulletin, —The female suffragists have been
raying things to Premier Dibbs. Now I want you
to let me say some things to the female suffragists,
I would ask you to say them, but I am afraid you
wouldn’t, as you are a bit of a female suffragist
yourself. Not that I feel any hostility to the
woman who wants a vote, or wants to sit in Par-
-'lament: because, after all, I’m not sure that you
are ui.it right in pushing the representation prinei-
pie to the point of one person one vote. Still, I do
want to say two or three things, and if you will let
■me, here goes.
• # *
My dear good women, God ordained that we
should all be fools, more or less : and you have no
call to exert yourselves to improve on His handi-
work. Please don’t talk about the Hand of God
driving you out to secure 'political power, so that
you may redeem the world. You can’t redeem
the world _by merely putting an end to the
shocking sights that you say are to be seen
in city streets. Of course, by “shocWng
sights ” you mean the women who congregate
there. Now, frankly, I don’t think it quite right
to use that term in such a sweeping fashion. So
far as outside appearance goes, some of them
really look very well. Besides, you won’t get all
those women off the streets just by giving them
•votes. You can’t put them all into Parliament.
# » *
Next, if you are really anxious to turn these
ladies into voters and possible members of Parlia-
ment, please don’t put it on the ground that they
have a right to it. Nobody has a right to the
suffrage, except as the law gives it to them. The
democratic principle of manhood suffrage is not
based on any notion that every man has a right to
vote, it is simply the means by which democracy
seeks to achieve its ends. If those ends could be
better achieved by the appointment of a dictator
or a Czar, there is nothing in the principle of de-
mocracy to prevent that plan from being adopted.
* * *
Government is not, and never has been, founded
on rights—except the right of the strongest. In
all ages the crowd that had the power has Used it
to carry out its own purposes. The source of
power has been constantly varying, but the rule
has always been vested in that man or body of
men who, as a matter of fact, were able to subju-
gate the rest. Sometimes a man has been boss
merely because he was the biggest and the
strongest, sometimes because he was the cleverest,
sometimes because he had the power of supersti-
tion or inherited ideas at his back. There have
been autocracies, and democracies, and theocracies,
and aristocracies, and the simple, old-fashioned
Government by a gang of swashbucklers ; but the
same principle has inspired them all. The man
who held the whip has always used it to make the
team go in the direction he wanted. When there
has been what is called a good king, what he
wanted has sometimes been to see the people
under him happy, and he has tried according to
his lights to get it —not because the people had a
right to it, but because it was what he wanted.
If what he had happened to want was somebody
else’s wife, he would have used his power to get
that.
« * #
Now the people are beginning to get the power
into their own hands. They haven’t got it alto-
gether yet. because they have not quite freed
themselves from the yoke of certain superstitious
and deeply-rooted modes of thought. But they
will get it, and when they do they will use it, as
all other ruling bodies have used it, to get what
they want. It is not quite clear yet what they
will want. The Bulletin thinks they will want
Socialism, I don’t. But whatever they do want
they will see that they get it. And the form of
Government they adopt will merely be the means
they choose to employ for getting it. If repre-
sentative government on a basis of manhood
suffrage commends itself as an efficient means,
they will adopt that. But if they do adopt it, it
will not be because every man has a right to the
suffrage; it will be because the democratic crowd
is the buss crowd, and that is the best way it can
see of getting what it wants,
* * *
So, my dears, if you want the suffrage, and
want to give it to the “shocking sights” of
King and George streets, don’t waste your breath
talking about rights. King Democracy is getting
himself settled on the throne, and your plan is to
make friends with him. Just find out what he
wants, and make him understand that the easiest
way for him to get it is to give you" votes, and
depend upon it, whether you have a right to them
or not, he will give them to you.
THE PLATYPUS.
According to “ Spectre,” inS.M. Herald,
South Sea natives don’t care to work on their own
island, “having enough to eat, they only con-
descend to do an odd job now and then.” When
they get away from home they have no choice.
“Spectre” shows this, and gives away the Queens-
land labour-question when he writes :
I asked one gentleman why he did not employ the men
at hand, instead of sending so far for other blacks. His
answer was significant. “See that fellow,” he said,
pointing to an intelligent black-boy who was acting as our
guide; “if I asked him to work for me, he would not
come for less than 4s. or ss. a day, and then he would only
come when he chose, and would be taking constant holi.
days. On the other hand, the men I brought here are
hound over to work for three or five years, and cannot
leave me during that time.”
Dear Bulletin, —In last week’s issue your
correspondent, “T.,” disposes of the Woman
Suffrage question in one act thus :
It is peculiarly unfortunate for the New South Wales
Women’s Suffrage League that there should, simul-
taneously with their demonstrative deputation to Premier
Dibbs, demanding the suffrage, have been displayed an
exhibition of woman’s work, The exhibition furnishes
the most crushing conceivable proof of women’s incom-
petence to participate in serious affairs.
I fully agree with the above. Bub at the same
time isn’t it somewhat regretable that the One-
man-one-vote question should come up at this
time, just when the bursting of 42 banks, and the
horrible collapse of the maritime strike, and the
shearers’ strike, and the Barrier miners’ strike,
and the inception of the fatuous Argentine
movement, have furnished the most crushing
proof that the capitalists of Australia are born
swindlers, and that the workmen are mostly fools ?
I have seen the show of women’s work for the
Chicago Exhibition, and it is simply ghastly, and
proves that women shouldn’t have any political
power whatever. I haven’t seen the exhibition of
men’s work for the same affair, because there isn’t
one. The N.S. W. exhibits consist almost entirely
of mineral specimens, wool, timber, and similar
raw material, and as to anything beyond it is
practically non est. This shows distinctly that
men shouldn’t have any political power either,
and a careful consideration of the Post-office
carvings, Hansard, Bishop Smith, the Balmain
ferry-boats, the Bare Island fort, Jawbone Neild’s
Bill for the suppression of Vice, the Y.M.C.A.,
the Federation Bill, the Church, the National Ass,
the recent Art Exhibition, the larrikin, and
nearly every other exclusively male product all
through Australia lends additional weight to the
contention. Why not carry the thing to its logical
conclusion ? A few women produce a most
inartistic collection of work—therefore, women are
incompetent to participate in serious affairs. Also,
a few men produce a fearsome collection of daubs
and call the same an art exhibition—therefore,
men in general arc incompetent to participate in
serious affairs either. —Yours, Ac,, Tilts Salt.
Malvolio Wins!
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Though there’s many a slip
’Twixt the Cup and the “ tip,’
And ’tis folly to dip
In a deep sea of sorrow,
Yet one cannot forget
That “ Our betters ” will bet,
Spite of certain regret
On the sober to-morrow.
And for once in a way
We take part in the play
Where ’the bookmakers lay
Little traps for the backers,
Who venture and dare
To rush into the snare
Till their pockets are bare
As those naked Kanakas.
Our war-cry is heard—
We have each a “ dead-bird ”
And a notion absurd
That it’s going to do it.
We ate dashing and bold,
Also green as of old,
And our bright yellow gold
Well, we manage to “ bloo ’’ it.
Vet protest is vain—
E’en the wise are insane
When the Cup comes again
With its vanities hollow
So, prithee, suppose
That the rooster who crows
Is “a party as knows,”
And “ a safe ’un to follow.”
Then, since each mother’s sou
Seems to back “ number one ”
In the contest begun
When his relatives bore him,
Number I (take the hint)
On the card is a gint,
For nothing in print
Takes position before him.
And Malvolio’s name
Wears the halo of Fame
He’s the very self-same
Lump of mettle and vigour
Who took the big trick
When he wasn’t as quick
As to-day, or as thick
In his thoroughbred figure.
What’s a stone, more or less,
To His Nibs ? We confess
A belief in success,
Which is why we are pinning
Our faith to a horse
That has galloped the course ;
And we think he’ll endorse
The opinion by winning.
You may, if you please,
Put a bit on Cor reze —
But certainly he’s
Not the stamp of Malvoly.
The boss of the pair
Is the big ’un, we swear,
You may follow us there,
Yea, by all that is holy !
As for clever G’Naroo,
All he’s going to do
May be perfectly true,
And we fancy his breeding ;
While The Admiral may
Be the sailor they say—
But you’ll find on the day
That Malvolio’s leading.
Young Oamoola can fly
As you’ll see by-and-bye ;
“ Clonard 1 ” they will cry
When some jockeys are whipping ;
And we fancy Portsea
Will be second (D.V.),
But to give two or three
Is ridiculous tipping.
For one only can win
(A “dead-heat” reckoned in)
Though a mob of them spin
For the glory and siller;
And we go for one straight,
Never heeding his weight.
Didn’t Redleap, of late,
Bring the grist to the Miller ?
The field for next week
Reads uncommonly weak—
Aye, in poorness unique.
Then is Carbine forgotten ?
The weight on Old Jack
(He’s a goner, alack !)
Was a flea on his back
When the others were rotten.
And here is a case
Where a lot in a race
Cannot live at the pace
That Correze means to make it.
But Malua’s stout son
Will be game for a run
When the duffers are “ done ”
And he’s going to “ shake it.”
EDMUND FISHER.
“Oh Liberty, what rhymes are committed
in thy name!”
An Irish physician recommends people to
boil their water and their ice.
They now put detective microphones into
the cells with Russian political prisoners.
Eloquent paragraph from Melbourne
Standard: “The finances of the Y.M.C.A. are
in a straitened condition, consequent on the
reverses in the fortunes of some of its erstwhile
chief supporters ”
Let’s lift our voices, sob aloud in sympathetic chorus,
And wail together of the woes that lately have come o’er
us.
The many pious gentlemen in whom the young men
trusted
Have paid for pounds with pennies, or incontinently
“ busted.”
Their pious prospect’s blighted, and our hopes are dis
appointed.
The hand of dire depression is upon the Lord’s anointed,
It seems the gay Y.M.C.A. must shape for liquidation,
Or down upon its marrow bones, and pray for more
inflation.
There’s sorrow in the Bourke-st. Hall, o’er pleasures that
are banished,
O’er the tea, and buns, and picnics, and the other com-
forts vanished.
Yet hope’s eternal—when once more their lucky star has
risen,
The holy cause will flourish when the chiefs arc cut of
prison.

“Thomas Bridle” ; Tub Bulletin never did suggest that
Ihe unions should be bossed by a "despot.” It suggested
that there should be a leader and a council, both elective,
and district councils, also elective—that there should be
a republican organisation instead of anarchy, and that
the leaders shouldn’t habitually give away the plans of
the unions to their enemies on the gas-bag principle. This
isn’t despotism, but level-headed self-government. [Many
replies held over.]

good billet to be had at Sydney registry-offices is
generally sold to the one who will pay most for it,
ability not being taken into consideration. At
present one without friends or money stands as
much chance of drawing a big sweep ns of getting
■employment in Sydney.

The man who having hot detects himself
singing before a race is doomed to lose. His only
safeguard is hedging. Few betting men have the
courage to wager their money after having seen a
cross-eyed person. Such an occurrence is an
omen of the worst sort. The appearance of a grey
horse never fails to cause anxious search for the
red-haired crirl —the combination is invincible. Tt
is a sure sign of success when a man keeps his
securities in the Sydney Safe Deposit.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Oct. 26, 1892.
My Dear Moorabinda, —
U ill religion become the rage in Sydney after Lady
Jersey’s sermonette the other day at theY.VV.C.A. tea
and hymn orgy, I wonder? After prizes had been
awarded for a “ Scripture-hunt ” (whatever that may be)
“ M.E.J.” rose and preached the Gospel like a brand-new
curate. Strikes me it’s a serious thing when a Governor’s
wife exhorts her subjects to shake off the Evil One,
because whatever m’ lady does must be the straight snip,
and if it’s the Government House caper to be in a saved
state of soul, Potts Point has got to be saved at once
before the next “At ’Ome.” It will be a tough contract
for most of 'em, but they’ll have to get grace somehow.
After all, though, methinks Lady J. keeps her religious
patter for the pouah peepul. It’s so nice to encourage
working-girls to embrace a moral faith that keeps them
out of mischief if they take it properly ; and it helps the—
er—department of the Benevolent Asylum to get its
second wind. You don’t hear many posers from the
Scriptures in the red droring-room at Government
’Ouse, and thougli M.E.J. couldn’t tear herself
away from the bun-struggle hereinbefore-mentioned ere
the young persons had voiced another hymn, 5 - et you
don’t see the “Sweet By-and-bye” down on the per-
fumed programme at her ladyship’s musical at homes.
Doesn’t Flossie Seemore want her frivolous little soul
saved as well as Mary Ann? Yet you never hear of Lady
J. rising with a solemn expression to talk about the
“practical way to advance the Kingdom” to a draw-
ing-room full of “Pointers.”
A fight to a finish proceeds at the charming Sydney
marine suburb of Bondi between a recalcitrant Anglican
flock and the Primate. The flock don’t want the angtl
in embryo whom the Primate has pitchforked among
them as their pastor. To definitely express their dis"
approval of this particular guide to a Better Land, the
deacons resigned in a body, and got the rest of the audi-
ence to stop away on the first Sunday and let fie parson
be brought to his bearings by an empty house.
But one member (a wealth j'merchant) kept true to S.
Smith (I don’t mean myself, but the bishop). He went
forth, and by dint of strong prayers and mild bribery he
got a scratch mob to fill the church for the parson’s
premiere. And when the bone of contention stepped
into his eyry he faced a cordial S.R.O. house, no matter
if Half of it was holding its church-seivice upside-down !
The only revenge left to the other side is to turn off the
gas at the meter during “ Evensong.”
The feature of the vice-regal garden-party last week
was the nigger-minstrelsy sort of figure a society lady
cut. You have seen those funny corner-men who come
out in evening-dress reversed—white where it ought to
be black, and vice versa? Well, that’s Just what she
looked like. Her skirt and coat were of white drill, with
a black silk shirt. Lots of rather gaudy frocks sported at
the garden-party suggested a trial canter before Melbourne
Cup.
Southern Society has been rather disappointed in the
wife of a recently-imported military Mogul, who has
turned out to be too pious to play chaueys with the vessels
of wrath. No society business for her. She wouldn’t
bare her shoulders for worlds, and if you do catch her
“moping mum” amidst gay and festive surroundings,
you may wager gloves that she’s strictly “official.” Al!
this is very disheartening to an upper one hundred whose
social wings have been clipped by departures for the
Continong, mourning insolvencies, and all the rest of the
visitations which society is heir to.
Sydney has an Amateur Banjo and Guitar Society, con-
sisting of thirty-one assorted members and a man to shake
a stick at them, and they gave a concert in the Centenary
Hall the other evening. The society looked very flowery,
for the fifteen ladies on deck wore radiant hues instead of
the idiot-like uniform of cream or white (occasionally
varied with a badge) which is the way the average con.
ductor drapes his ladies when they let him. Though, as
a general thing, the neutral tints are the safest, because
you run fearful risks of Jarring colour-contrasts on a
chromotrope stage. I have a painful recollection of a
crimson plush getting next to a red tulle on a platform
once and wrecking my evening.
The Society’s music didn’t exactly irritate me. Some
of the effects were sweet though wiry—like a Jew’s harp in
the hand of a master, while sometimes you couldn’t help
being reminded of a stick rattl ng i.lrng an iron fence
for the mantle of D’Arcy Read lias not yet fallen on the
amiteur guitar-ites. But there aren’t many musica
possibilities in the average “ chitarro” outside of moon-
light gipsyings, where even the concertina-squeaker is
treated like an artist. The Society’s best numbers were
the “Sylvia” pizziati and a couple of old Spanish
marches, both of which came in for some of the encores
that telescoped the fifteen items out to a couple of dozen.
Of the eight people put down to assist the amateurs
Miss Maud Fitz-Stubbs, at the piano, came off best. Her
light, firm touch and genius for clear-cut pianissimo got
a double call for “Marcia,” and an artistic little bit was
given in response—Just the right-sized encore, by the way,
to wedge into a crowded bill. And encores remind me
that I would like to warn any screechy soprano whose
vocal steam - whistle is courteously tolerated by the
audience not to be too eager to return and give out
another burst of song as long as a Dutch hotel bill, Just
because somebody is clapping. The wily singing woman
who wants to be popular should keep her eye to a hole in
the curtain or somewhere, so as to see whether all the
enthusiasm isn’t due to the exertions of a loyal small
brother in a conspicuous position.
I have often been vastly entertained (while listening to
amateur music) by the airs and graces of the performer
who mounts the stage, wearing his hair long and his
collar short, and a yearning expression of countenance,
because he always has to steer for the hind-most seat.
■The more neck and lack of barber-ism an amateur puts
on the more you can safely bet on seeing him cheek-by-
Jowl with the eighth fiddle.
This, really happened the other day in the whalebone
department of a big Sydney drapery. An unmistakeable
lady hayseed, accompanied by a small girl, wandered up
to the counter and demanded “ a pair of staiys for this
one.” “What size does the little one take ?” asked the
attendant, naturally enough, but this ordinary query sent
the green-hide mamma completely to grass. After a pain-
ful pause and much mental wrestling a bright idea
occurred. “ I dunno’ exactly,” she said, “ but ’er dada’s
six foot in is socks if that’ll ’elp yer.”
“ An influential committee ” intend giving Mayor and
Mayoress Manning a complimentary return ball on 15th
Nov. in the Centennial Hall. They really ought to give it
on the sth Nov., because the company will all look regular
guys after the first waltz in such a summery month.
However, the date is fixed, and Messrs. John Woods and
Leonard Dodds have been appointed hon. secs.
Talking about hot weather dances, if we could only
come out in the airy twilight ascribed to a Brisbane belle
at a recent Banana ball, I’m sure we could all dance right
through the dog days. Her description doesn’t take
more than a line—“ Miss— —, a tasteful salmon pink
sash.” That’s all; and from the simple admiration ex-
pressed by the reporter, one gathers that the lady was the
belle of the evening.
The two recent sensational, sentimental Sydney suicides
show up the opposite effect of love on masculine and
feminine natures better than whole books of Goethe-isms.
Everybody knows the particulars. The man had been
jilted by a girl. One night he followed her home, and
after some overbearing, useless words, he shot her twice,
and then fled into the darkness with a yell and shot
himself dead.
The other was a young girl, who left a melo-dramatie
shilling novelettey letter by the railway-line, and then
calmly placed herself in front of a train. The letter said
(it was all obviously cribbed from a shocker) —“Forget-
fulness of him is not in my heart, and remembrance of
him will cease only with death. Again I charge you to
tell him all, but only in the event of my premature death.
Make no mistake, dear.” Could you draw a clearer
definition between man’s fierce, selfish, egotistical passion
and woman’s meek, self-renouncing love?
A concert in aid of the furnishing fund of Prince
Alfred Hospital nurses’ home happened in the Centennial
Hall, on Monday evening, under the direction of Rivers
Allpress. The Governor brought Lady Hilda Brode-
rick (in a rather dejected pale-blue silk), who
behaved very gracefully over the beautiful committee
roses meant for Lady Jersey—it’s rather difficult to do
the stalking-horse act prettily; the Jersey governess
in black and blue (she was the recipient of a flat
little nosegay like a tart) and Captain Leigh. There
was a light, though creamy, attendance, and most of the
damsels had their little semi-chignons tied up with wide
ribbons ending in a flat bow at the top. This, by the
way, is a very kind style for the hairless houri who can
dilute her scanty tresses with radiant ribbons and get up
a head like an “ after using ” hair-restorer ad. The stage
was patriotically trimmed with waratahs, palms, and so
on, and some draped bunting helped the acoustics
muchly.
A feature of the concert was the musicianly reading
given by Miss Lottie Hyam (in a smart white satin gown),
and Rivers Allpress of a Grieg sonata for piano and violin.
But Miss Hyam alone in a Lizst “ Rhapsodie ” displayed
certain mannerisms in the first part which prejudiced the
whole bit. Miss O’Reilly’s “ Old and Young Marie ” was
feelingly voiced, but here and there she produced an ex-
plosive consonant with a force that spoilt the artistic
aspect of her song. And Arthur Deane wobbled. This
budding baritone has a gold-mine in his throat if he pro-
spects for it, only he mustn’t be content to go on as a
pleasing amateur who doesn’t deserve a brick even though
he pumps his music up and down in the agonies of vocali-
sation as if the sheet were a beer-engine. Strauss, a
remnant of the dead and gone Orpheus Club (very much
out of form as might be expected), and Miss E. Deane
were the rest of the stars dotted about the pea-green pro-
gramme.
Yours affectionately.
SAPPHO SMITH.
The Wild Cat Column.
The Barrier Miner (Broken Hill, N.S.W.)
still holds out a shadowy hope of success for the miners
on strike. It is about time to abandon this delusion.
The only prospect is the maintenance of a passive resist-
ance, and passive resistance is not tearing up the rails
which, metaphorically speaking, has been tried and has
failed, but simply lying down on the track when the train
is coming. It is the same thing as attempting to burst
the Union Bank by persistently sitting on the doorstep.
It is the art of conducting war by not fighting and not
running away, but only standing still and waiting to be
captured. The mines are steadily filling with blackleg
labour, and in the present ghastly state of depression it is
certain that the supply of blacklegs is almost unlimited.
Tlie miners, with ail the resources of the law against
them, cannot eject these intruders by force ; they can
only treat them witli silent contempt. And the blackleg
doesn’t care a straw for silent contempt.
# * #
. Mr. Whippingdale Johnson, P.M ~ wires to
Sydney that “it may yet be necessary for the Govern-
ment to protect the unionists from the fury of the
workers”—i.e., the blacklegs. This is Mr. Whippingdale
Johnson’s satirical method of saying that the strike is
over.

It is alleged that the same Diobs Cabinet which
so enthusiastically suspended the labour conditions for
the big silver companies at Broken Hill, when they broke
their arbitration agreement, positively, refused a similar
suspension for a small flux co., which was unable to carry
on because the Barrier trouble had stopped the demand
for its product.
