“ Seller, 245.; no buyer,” is the latest published
quotation for Federal Bank shares. These shares are 50s.
paid-up; the dividend lias been omitted this half-year;
and the last stiver of the £135,000 reserve-fund has been
set aside to meet “ possible shrinkages in securities.”
The trail of James Munro is over tire Federal, but surely
that alone isn’t sufficient to explain all this?
%
The Royal Bank of Australia held its meeting
in Melbourne the other day and declared a dividend of
5 per cent. The Royal Bank has been in existence for
about five years, its paid-up capital is £300,000, and its
reserve fund is exactly £5OOO, which is about as remark-
able an instance of thrift as that of tire man who lias two-
pence put away as a provision for his old age. Also the
auditors say that “ in our opinion ” the balance-sheet is a
true and correct statement-a reservation which is worth
noting.
# # #
At the Mercantile Bank meeting Graham
Berry remarked, more in sorrow than in anger, that if he
had known the smash was coming he would have sold his
shares, and as he didn’t sell them, that was sufficient
evidence that he was kept in profound ignorance. Which,
put into simple language, ii just this: “I drew my
dividends while 1 could get them, and took a_ big salaiy
as director to watch over the interests of the shareholders
and depositors, I obviously didn’t watch over them,
though paid to do it, therefore I didn’t know that a
smash was coming - . If 11 ad known, I, the paid guardian
Of the shareholders* interests, would have used my posi-
tion to get rid of my own liability, and pass the ruin
on to somebody else. Darn everybody else ! Possibly
some widow would have ben hfc my shares and been
beggared. However, that would have been her funeral,
not mine. Eat or be eaten ! Amen ! ”

* * *
Some years back a N.S.W. Western District
squatter had a good quartz-mine. The reef was fairly big
and averaged an oz. In the vicinity, some miners struck
a 2ft. reef and carried a shaft down on it 100 feet. The
crushings taken out of the shaft averaged an oz. The
squatter waited and watched developments, and finally
■came along with an offer of £2OOO. It was accepted.
Strange to say, he could never get out of that reef more
than 5 dwts. to the ton, which would not pay, and having
lost his money, finally abandoned the mine. Explanation
—some shareholders with the miners worked in the
squatter’s other claim, from which they took away enough
good stone to make the other average an oz., and thus by
his own gold was the squatter “salted.”
* * *
The value of all evidence, at public meetings as
in Law Courts, can only be estimated by the proved
■credibility, or otherwise, of the giver. Nobody, as far as
The Bulletin has observed, show s a disposition to cheer
the deeply incriminated prisoner who makes a plain state-
ment of his innocence from the dock.
# * #
John Robb, of the Melbourne Herald, says
that if his Co. loses the action against the Standard
Co., the case will be taken to the Full Court, and, if
necessary, to the Privy Council.
# * #
A Melbourne manufacturing co. was floated at
boom time, conjointly in Melbourne and London, at a
figure rather larger than its proper value. After two
years’ work, dividends fell so much that the Melbourne
shareholders, representing about three-quarters of the
total number, called a meeting and agreed to wind-up
the co. and reorganise—present shareholders to have pre-
ference of allotment in the new concern. Result—
Melbourne shareholders receive decent divs. while London
ditto get back about one-tenth of their cash invested.
This is one way of restoring the confidence of the British
investor.
# * #
It is not an unmixed blessing to have a pushing
broker like Arthur Forbes exploiting Australian mines
•on the London market. The mines he has introduced so
far have been good in themselves, but in the transfer to
English investors they become watered unto attenuation.
Take, for instance, the Golden Gate, Charters Towers.
The capital of the company in Australia was £12,000, in
48,000 shares of ss. It lias now been converted into an
English Co., capital £48,000 in 96,000 shares of 10s., exactly
four times the original amount. Now who bags the differ-
ence?

The well-worn gag of election-time, about the
strike being the cause of the depression, is never heard in
Melbourne now. Even the most rampant young Vic.-
torians join in the general denunciation of Davies and Co.
as the authors of the trouble.
* # #
The old Solitary mine at Wattle Flat, N.S.W.,
has lately been taken up ; an effort is being made to float
it in Bathurst. Much gold was won from it years ago,
chiefly by tributors, who paid 15 per cent, royalty. Since
then the old battery has been broken up, the winding-
plant rendered useless, and the main shaft is full of water.
The reef is said to average 18in., and used to run 14dwts.
to an ounce per ton. It will take £4OOO to put the con-
cern in good working order, and if the men of Bathurst
put it up, they will be’ plucky.
# # #
Alter many months’ delay, the Alpine Pioneer
claim has been given to the miners who took possession
by virtue of their miners’ rights. It is within a mile of
Victorian border, near Delegate. A number of crushings
have averaged lOozs. per ton. The vein is small, hut well
defined between two splendid walls several feet apart, and
which, with depth, will probably fill with quartz, The
run of gold has been opened up on the surface over 50
yards in length. All the crushings have been taken on
the surface, hence there is only one reservation to make—
depth. In length it is a good thing ; depth has not been
proved, but should the gold go down as far as it is proved
in length (over 150 feet), this mine will make a big name.
At present it is a fair speculation.
# * *
Sydney D.T. must surely have changed its
Pambula correspondent. For many months we have
heard news of wonderful discoveries, big lodes, rich in
gold, prospective and continuous dividends. All lias
changed, and the D.T. now sings our tune, and condemns
everything but the field. The writer has condemned
everything, the field included. Bad as management lias
been the failure of the field is attributed to one potent
cause—insufficient gold. The ore bodies are not fissure
lodes but irregular, ill-defined masses of rock in which
gold is sometimes rich but always precarious and treacher-
ous. All the old excuses are played out.
s # #
N.S.W. Government Geologist Pittmann con-
cludes that the formation and origin of the Broken Hill
lodes is analogous to the saddle gold reefs of Bendigo, i.e.,
a cap with an eastern and western leg; and that like
Bendigo, when these legs taper away with depth, other
caps and legs will lie found. This coiild be proved or dis-
proved by boring through the cap to a greater depth—
which he advises.
# # *
The report of the official receiver on the Dr
Kaap gold mine, South Africa, shows how the British
mining investor is “let in.” The public paid £BO,OOO
cash, some of which went thus Underwriting, £lO,OOO ;
nominal vendor, £10,000; brokers, £1000; law costs,
£1400; advts., £1000; promoter, £25,000. And the
manager, unable to get his salary, got a Judgment, and
the whole of the Co.’s property was sold for £172, the
machinery, which alone cost £5OOO, and had never been
unpacked, being knocked down for £B5. Reminds one of
N.S.W.!
# # #
Several miners and tradesmen have lately
written asking for our opinion of the chief South African
centres from the worker’s standpoint. When we last
heard from Johannesburg the place swarmed with dis-
appointed men from all parts, mostly Americans and Aus-
tralians, preparing to quit. The' ore was nearly all
low'-grade—from 5 to lOdwts.—and only extensive up-to-
date machinery enabled most of them to carry on at all.
Very few were paying dividends. Business was dull.
“ The trail of the late boom was over the land and the
labour-market was drugged,” wrote our correspondent.
That was a little more than a month ago. The whole
place is in the hands of British syndicates, and all things
are made to work together for their good as far as pos-
sible. Prom which we infer that the Australian worker
can’t improve his lot in South Africa.
* • *
Three tons of tin-ore—only three tons and
nothing more—were lately rooted out of a mine at Omeo
(Vic.). The result of this brilliant success is recorded in
the local paper : —“ A public holiday was proclaimed and
great enthusiasm was manifested in the event. A very
large gathering assembled from all parts of the district.
The procession, headed by the town band, started from
the company’s office and marched through the town. A
photograph was taken before the waggon started. The,
town was gaily decorated with flags and banners, most
conspicuous being the banner on the waggon, hearing the
inscription, ‘Three tons on board, three millions to fol-
low.’ ” The report further states that the shareholders
had good reason to be proud. Fireworks were let off in
the evening, and Omeo got tolerably drunk. We are now
waiting to hear which liquidator has been appointed.
* * #
A provincial Tasmanian bankrupt called a meet-
ing of creditors in his lawyer’s office. The clock therein
registered Hobart time, owing to which mistake the
lawyer (holding 10 proxies) and the bankrupt alone were
present. Proxy 10 moved and Proxy 2 seconded the
solicitor into the chair; bankrupt placed himself unre-
servedly in the hands of the meeting, but could make no
offer ; Proxy 8 moved and Proxy 3 seconded the appoint-
ment of the chairman as trustee—unanimously carried.
“ Any other business, gentlemen ?” “None ; ” Proxy 7
moved and Proxy 4 seconded a cordial vote of thanks to
the chair—carried without dissent; chairman thanked the
meeting, which broke up harmoniously Just as an adverse
creditor, with 15 proxies, appeared at the door, and was
told—" The little matter is settled, the meeting has dis-
persed, the statute does not permit it to be re-opened.”
The Supreme Court has upset the arrangement, apolo-
gising, however, for its broad interpretation of the Act.
# # #
A five-ton lot of stone, showing nice gold, is
being treated in Brisbane for a party working a reef at
Enoggera, a few miles out. The Brisbane papers notice
the thing favourably and head-line it with “Anticipated
Good Yield.” The Enoggera reefs ought to be known to
Brisbane people as rare old stringers. Neheir.iah Bartley
could tell all about them, if a generation hath arisen that
knows not the history of the Enoggera. Gold in the
quartz was found there as far hack as ’6B, if not earlier,
and a lot of work in sinking and tunnelling done. The
reefs expand and pinch, and the gold comes in in splashes
and then runs out again. No money lias ever been made
out of Enoggera. Besides, all Brisbane stands on an
auriferous formation, barring the alluvial flats along the
river, where tropical scrub used to flourish. There is a
gold-bearing reef of the Enoggera quality at Petrie’s Bight,
which was exposed in a cutting right under what was Dr.
Hobbs’ house. Not far from Wickham Terrace, i.e.,
almost in the heart of the town, there is a cottage where
the landlord put down a shaft on a reef. All the ridges at
the back of South Brisbane were once pitted with pro-
spectors’ holes, made by hard-up men who got stuck in
Brisbane on their way from the South to the Gympie rush.

The Witwatersrand (South Africa) gold-mines
are now turning out over IOO.OOOoz. per month. The
total output of the field for 1888, was 230,1890 z. ; for 1883,
369,5510 z. ; for 1890, 494,8100 z. ; and for 1891, 729,2330 z. :
while for the first half of the current year.the total yield
has reached 562,7040 z. The output lias ben practically
doubled during the last 12 months, the increase for June
last over the corresponding month of the previous year
being about 48,0000 z. The monthly totals for the current
year have been : January S 4,. r >6ooz., February 80,6190 z.,
March 93,2440 z., April 95,5620 z., May 99,4360 z., June
103,2530 z., and July, a little less. All the gold of the
earth doesn't come from our country.

“Joan of Arc ” ends her career at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) this week, so it is needless to
use much further strong language about the ill-
starred female. “Joan” closes the season for
good, and if the Gaiety girls ever pay another
visit to Australia, The Bulletin trusts that they
will put a little more variety into their hair, which
has been one of the weak points in a show that
was rather exhausted all along. A company
whose hair is moveable should adapt the thatch to
the climate. In these last few days of the exhi-
bition the company, barring Lonnen, Courtneidge,
Haslem, and, perhaps, one more, shows a ten-
dency to fly to pieces a good deal. Miss Marion
Hood, who has developed an inclination to
fall asunder all through the season, is looking a
trifle more careless than usual, and the feminine
part of the company is kept up principally by the
vigour of the long, silent girl, who so recently
burst forth into song and speech. The impression
they create is that the female end of the crowd is
pretty tired of Australia, and will be glad to get
away. This continent was a gorgeous place when
they visited it for the first time, but the johnnies
of those days stood the champagne mostly out of
the depositors’ money, and now that the banks
are “ broke,” and the chappies are in gaol, it is a
cold, depressing land, dry and dead-broke, and
full of melancholy. .
“La Fille de Madame
Angot,” at Sydney Garrick ou
Saturday, pointed the moral that
the old school of comic-operetta
wants to have its talky dreary
dialogue cut up into half-inch
colloquialisms of the latter-day
libretto before people will be
trampled to death round the brok-
ing-office. A comic opera in
which a baggy villain stands on
an empty stage and hurls stilted
English at a ter-raitor, in a,
deep bass voice, wants gags as
badly as King Billy wants bacca,
Tis true you often want to kill
an incurable gagster, but even
that is better than going to
sleep. Soldene the She of the
operatic stage—as Madame Lange
presented a blooming appearance
when she didn’t come too near
the pitiless footlights, and got
thro’ her music on the very
fag end of a once-liquid warble;
yet her numbers were most
pleasing especially her tender
rendering of '“I Love Him So.”
Miss Deakin’s Clairette got on
very well all through. Johnny
Gqurlay’s “ Bomponnet ” was a
bright spot in a very spotted
performance his “She is so
Innocent” deserved the howling
encore that chased it. Also Rosa
Conroy was a picturesque fish-fag
and put good elbow-business into
the “legend” tho’ her voice ia
too light for her to do much harm
as a vocalist. The chorus deserve
a cheer, too, for the amount of in-
terest they put into their work,
from the Greek girl who pulled
her skirts higher to show more
of her boots, to the conspirator
who banged his enthusiastic orow-
bar into some concealed crockery on the prompt
side. Also Bella Bashall’s serpentine dance
received a call and a floral snake, twining round
a stick, which Bella held in her arms and kissed
as you see gushy girls kiss a baby when there’s a
bachelor present.
** * *
George Rignold, that old favourite with
the yearning expression beneath the limelight,
opens at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday in
the “Silver King.” George himself plays the
part of Wilfred Denver, and among his supporters
are Edward Sass, Hans Phillips, Harry Shine,
Misses Henrietta Watson, Watts-Phillips and
others. The scenery, by Gordon and Goatchei’, is
being laid on regardless of expense.
** * *
Sundry variety shows keep on their way
with much the same success as hitherto. The
variety co. seems proof against change; it
doesn’t boom much in good times, and it doesn’t
fade to any appreciable extent when times are
bad. Dan Tracey’s minstrel troupe at Sydney
Gaiety still retains the lead. The two Aquariums
(Bondi and Ooogee), the Alhambra, and a few
other places also announce new and stupendous
attractions at intervals, which generally means
that the man who used to be at the one show is
now at the other show instead. Also one new
and stupendous attraction is so like another that
very few people can tell the difference.
** * *
“ Walker, London,” was put before an
expectant public at Melbourne Bijou on Saturday
night. The curtain rose upon this new and ori-
ginal incentive to slow merriment somewhere
about 8 p.m,, and by 10.20 the story of “ Walker,
London,” had trickled away into silence, two
fairly long waits having occurred meanwhile.
Nobody would wish to speak harshly of this
entertainment. It doesn’t excite anything but
mirth, nor too much of that. It is an episode in
the mild career of a harmless barber, who obtrudes
himself into a select social circle on a house-boat,
and passes for somebody else. The girl of his
heart—a plump and relentless female in a green
sash —prowls around in pursuit of this uninterest-
ing man, until she finally falls asleep on the upper
deck of the house-boat. When she has slumbered
for half-an-hour, the tame haircutter hooks her
chair to a crane and swings the torpid Venus into
space, whereupon she wakes up and argues the point.
By this time the barber has tired of mixing in
house-boat society under false pretences, so he
lowers his girl into a punt below and the pair
return from whence they come under cover of the
stilly night. His fashionable friends, becoming
aware of this strange flight in the punt, cry aloud
for his address, and the distant voice of the barber
is heard to exclaim, “ Walker, London !” as the
tragedy concludes. This same punt is pushed to
and fro rather frequently during the evening.
The half-dozen leading characters are addicted to
going up-stream in the punt, and returning pre-
sently, but sometimes they vary the monotony by
sitting down on the tow-path. Two girls help to
fill up the time by becoming engaged to two young
men, except when they are lavishing a. casual
romantic affection upon the stray barber. Also,
there is an elderly mamma who devotes her life to
crochet, and a handmaiden who smashes the
dinner-plates. Eor the rest there is little but
silent river and the sleeping form of the spinster
in a green sash. J. M. Barrie, a popular humour-
ist of the “new school,” wrote “Walker, Lon-
don,” for J. L. Toole and Go., and it may be that
the ancient comedian is highly diverting in the
chief part. Robert Brough was not specially
comic on Saturday. He didn’t get inside his
character, nor did his character appear to have an
inside for him to get into. Misses Romer, Major,
Temple, and Gibson, together with Boucicault,
Cecil Ward, and Chichester, hovered around the
house-boat in their usual pleasing manner, and
Spong’s one bit of riverside scenery met with loud
approval from a crowded audience.
* * *
At the Opera House the Alabama Minstrels
are doing what good r iggers have ever done in a
"FALK,” PHOTOGRAPHER, SYDNEY,
MISS ALICE LEAMAR.
specially capable manner, and business flourishes.
The show continues to start off in a black semi-
circle and work along into clog dances and discus-
sions between Woods and Emerson, but fresh
ideas sprout forth as the season progresses. In
fact, the performance on the whole is inclined to
be better than thousands of others which have
gone before, and if the myriads of nigger-shows
yet to come should be a little better than this, it
will merely prove that the world is gradually
moving on.
** * *
Pollard’s troupe of highly-trained juveniles
are still enjoying themselves at the Princess’s,
“The Gondoliers ” which ran all last week, seems
to display these rising artists at their best, inas-
much as it is mostly under the control of the girls,
who sing with feeling and have nice little ways
with them. The boys, on the other hand, chortle
like bull-frogs and, as comedians, are but very
weak suggestions of men, after all. In “Mas-
cotte,” produced last Saturday, the lads Stephens
and Quealy had to do more than was good for
them. They were undoubtedly successful con-
sidering their age and sex, but the little damsels,
either in skirts or tights, seemed more at home on
the stage. Audran’s opera was to be followed on
Wednesday by “ The Mikado,” for three nights,
after which the collection of youth and inno-
cence will skip sorrowfully away. It is nob
generally known that these children love
acting more than pie. They hang on to
rehearsals with their teeth, and can with difficulty
be induced to leave off singing choruses when they
have sung enough. In fact, Pollard has to rush at
them and shout “Shssh ! ” several times before his
company will clear the stage.
** * *
On Saturday the Gaiety Burlesque crowd
appear at the Princess’s for their third Melbourne
season. Twice they have made a farewell appear-
ance at another theatre and promised to go away
for good, but hero they are again (or will be im-
mediately) like the gruesome clown of Xmas
memory. It is the intention of this oft-recurring
epidemic to break out anew in “Faust Up to
Date,” and a booking fee of half-a-orown will give
ecldt to the joyful occasion.
** * *
Other attractions are getting ready for
Derby night, and Melbourne is threatened with a
violent revival of music and the drama all over
the place. At the Royal they are polishing up
the furniture for Maggie Moore, who has hired
the scene of her former triumphs for positively
thirteen nights only, commencing as “Meg, the
Castaway.” A holiday company including Annie
Taylor, Corlesse, Geo. Carey, Greenaway, Charles
Brown, &c., are seized with a ndble ambition to
have another go at the Alexandra, so a fresh
chapter in the chequered career of that establish-
ment will be opened with “ Mixed.” Max O’Rell
returns to the Athenaeum for Cup week, and
Frank Clark has already returned to the Vic.
for an indefinite period. The Ooghills and Dan
Tracey are running their rival halls as usual, and
many other places are putting on a carnival spurt.
Altogether, Melbourne is trying to look very
giddy and light-hearted.
** * *
Armes Beaumont, who lately put his trust
in banks and lost his money, is to get a grand
benefit concert at Melbourne Town Hall on
Thursday (10th Nov.). It is hard luck for Beau-
mont to really need a benefit in the autumn of his
melodious life, and a host of old admirers, local
and up from the country, may be expected to
embrace this opportunity of assisting to “ tem-
per the wind ” to the shorn tenor.
For “Poverty Point," see page 20.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
For the first time in several years one feels
doubtful—writing a week before the race—of being able
to pick the Melbourne Cup winner in, say, six tries at the
post. Mentor, Bravo, Carbine, and Malvolio were each, in
.heir respective seasons, included amongst the popular
naif-dozen most likely ones, and no rational punter or
prophet left them entirely out of his calculations. But
just now, with no recently brilliant performers, save
Paris and Camoola, and no reliable stable tips to swear by,
it is hard to be wise before the event. Possibly there will
be good judges ready to back six against the field when
the numbers go up, but in the meantime thirteen horses
at least seem to have a show of winning, for the
reason that there is no line by which they can
be proved (on paper) quite out of the contest. Mal-
volio, G’Naroo, Camoola, Portsea, Correze, Malolo,
The Admiral, Paris, Candour, St. Albans 11., Tirail-
leur, Steadfast, and Penance, shall stand as The
Bulletin's thirteen, and the others can consider them-
selves rejected. Some of those others are perhaps worthy
of notice, but the writer takes the awful responsibility of
saying that such as Oxide, Honor Bright, Eurus, Affiance,
and Clonard, may be safely discarded for this year’s
Melbourne Cup.
* # *
Working backwards at the list of thirteen, then,
we will object to the heavily-backed and lightly-weighted
Penance on account of his want of size and loss of form
last year. Smart two-year-old colts that “go off ” as
they grow seldom come on again to any great extent.
Besides, Penance hasn’t grown much, and doesn’t
look equal to a two-mile journey. Steadfast, on the
other hand, has won over the distance, but if he is to pull
off the Melbourne Cup, he wdll have to start a fairly warm
favourite, and the public can wait for the Wilson money
to go on before thinking seriously about the son of
Nordenfelt and Nellie Moore. Steadfast is a grandly
bred one, who, fit and well at the post, would be the pick
of the handicap, bar nothing. Seemingly he isn’t fit and
well. Tirailleur also appears to be under the proverbial
“ cloud ” once more, although a few weeks ago Mr. Gollan
cherished hopes of this delicate horse. He was
better than he had been for the last twelve months, but
now the touts declare that Tirailleur is a “goner,” and
the touts are sometimes right. When wily Hebrews offer
100 to 3 against a high-class animal, the case looks bad.
So over goes Tirailleur.
* • #
St. Albans 11. is unfashionably bred, and nob
very brilliant as yet. The latest form of this horse is his
best form, all the same. Also, he seems good enough to
back against Forty Winks, wherever they may finish, and
many clever people think that Forty Winks will finish
nearly in fiont, notwithstanding his seedy aupearance
when he ran at Caulfield. Call St. Albans 11.‘“danger-
ous, and let those who know all about it risk their money
on him. Candour may win the Melbourne Cup if he wins
the Derby. His chance for the handicap comes on for
discussion under the head of Camoola.
* * *
Paris is presumably the fastest horse in the
whole crowd, and the most likely to be in front as they
turn the corner. He couldn’t stay the two miles last
year, though, a fact which, taken in conjunction with his
9st. 81b., justifies one in throwing him out this time, good
as he is. The term “ crushing weight ”is a relative one.
Little Paris may be crushed by an extra stone or so,
which, in the writer’s opinion, makes precious little differ-
ence to splendid cattle like Malvolio. Wherefore, over
goes Paris, and The Admiral with him. Mr. Sam Cook’s
nag is supposed to be very well again, and he ranks as a
Derby winner. But it was a poor Derby that The Admiral
won, and after winning it he finished behind Correze for
the Cup of ’9l. If Correze be equally well, as they say,
he certainly ought to beat The Admiral once more.
* » •
Malolo is a mysterious animal, almost the image
of his sire, Malua, but his chance is purely a matter of
stable estimate, and stable estimates are frequently in-
correct. Up to the present, Ike Foulsham has named
G’Naroo as the pick of his lot—whether this “informa-
tion ” indicates that Malolo can beat G’Naroo is a deep
question. In the last Melbourne Cup it happened that
G’Naroo, a strong favourite, fell over at a time when,
according to jockey Morrison, he had only to go
on and win. “ I was overhauling them easily when
the horse came down,” said Morrison to Mr. Ike.
But jockeys always fancy these things. G’Naroo,
whatever his people may think of the recent Caulfield
Cup running, isn’t a “dead bird” on paper, and no one
can trust him with boots and socks for the sake of what
he has done. His weight is the chief thing in his favour,
because other horses with much about the same handi-
cap haven’t done as much. Portsea is a game little
sticker who runs religiously behind in small events of late,
but is always being backed. Bamage declares that
Portsea is the best horse, bar Carbine, he ever rode, so, if
Bamage is a young man of brains, and strictly truthful
to boot, Portsea ought to go very near to it.
In a field where mature second - raters will rank
as giants, a good three • year - old must have
a great chance. Camoola’s excellence is still
open to doubt, and even a Derby win (we think he
will win the Derby) may not mean much this year.
Camoola ran home like a genuine article in the Caulfield
Stakes, though, and should he pull off the Derby with the
same graceful ease, nothing but want of stamina can
keep him out of a place for the Cup. Finally, we have
(at time of writing) Correze and Malvolio, backed at 10
and 12 to 1. The market points to Correze as the better
horse of the two, and all the “ knowing ” ones sav he is
better than last year’s winner. But didn’t people sav the
same of Tantallon, the stable-companion of Carbine, a
week before the great horse romped in ? Handicapper
Dakin fancies Correze for the Cup, and he is not the worst
informed man in Melbourne on racing matters. Yet, one
can’t see why the horse that won last year shouldn’t win
again in a worse field this. Malvolio ought to be quite as
lit as Correze, and the weight will scarcely stop him if he
is his old self on the day. Five shillings on Malvolio, and
chance it!
• # •
Like Bravo, winner of the ’1)0 Melb. Cup,
Clonard has twice successively run third for Caulfield
Cup.
* # #
In ’9l we tipped a horse they call Correze—
Twas a printer’s error dropped us in the stew ;
Now we fain your loss and anger would appease,
On our oath, we meant the brute for ’92.

form and in good condition, The Admiral is about the

There’s a bright little girl in a Sydney shop who
has backed the Derby and Cup winners straight out for
the past three years. She picks them herself, too, and is
particular about the price.
» * m
One Sydney tote-man says he tried hard to run
square, but could not get the public to patronise it until
he “faked." Another, well established, says he runs it
square because he is making a pile at it, and it’s too much
bother to fake.
* # #
In having two strings to his bow, Redfern has
the whiphand over the big sweepdrawers. For instance,
if the holder of the Malvolio ticket is not liberal, the
trainer and owner can jig Correze, and vice versa.
* * *

sweeps will total some £17,000. It would be interesting

game, in commission, not to speak of unclaimed prizes.
* * *
Also, what arrangement he has with the Bank
of N.S. Wales as to interest on the moneys paid into his
credit.

Morgan should be in the next N.S.W. inter-
colonial cricketing team. He is not far away from the

• # #
Ohilde Harold, the champion Sydney trotting
pony, the other day reared clean over and dashed his
brains out. He was just entering upon stud duty, and
his death will at all events prevent a deal of confusion in
the future in trotting pedigrees. The man who christened
the pony after his sire possessed little originality.
* * *
A lych gate, said to be one of the most beauti-
ful in England, has been erected at the entrance to
Lambourn Church, to the memory of Charles Jousiffe,
race-horse trainer. The Bishop of Reading performed the
dedicatory service.

British liars take the cake. Ninety-nine out of
every hundred Britishers who visit the kawlinies have
stroked the University eight of their year, and been
taught boxing by Jem Mace. The Australian liar is good
in his way, but he lacks the cast-iron cheek of the tucked-
up trousers brigade.

Some queer things happen in this world. A
big Dutchman, Owns, goes to England and wins the
Diamond Sculls at Henley; then Frenchman Lepron
comes along and beats Owns. Then a French eight beats
an eight from London Rowing Club. What is the matter
with J. Bull ?

James Mansfield, secretary of the Victorian
Seamen’s Union, has been fined £lO and heavy
costs “ for persuading a seaman to leave thes.s.
Gabo.” Now, supposing David Syme were fined
for persuading a pressman to leave the Argus and
join the Age instead—what kind of a row would
follow, we would like to know ?
Mr. Alexander Britton, who died at Syd-
ney on Monday, was one of the most ex-
perienced of Australian pressmen, and had for
the last year or two, since his secession from
the S.M. Herald, been engaged in the task of
preparing for publication, on behalf of the Govern-
ment, the early records of N.S. Wales, Mr.
Britton, who was only in his 47th year when he
passed away, spent the best years of his life in the
exhausting position of sub-editor of Melbourne
Argus, to which journal his younger brother, Mr.
Henry Britton, is still attached. The deceased
was as taciturn and as reserved as all the other
Argus men of long ago, but his quiet exterior
concealed a warm and manly heart, and he will
be sincerely mourned by his family and comrades.

Tom Curran,, the. ex-
pugilist, relates that when
he and Harry, Sallars were
prosecuted for their prize-
fight at, W.enibee, Aspinall
was briefed to defend him.
He began by panegyrising
the noble art of seif-defence,
and wound up a brilliant
speech with—“ Gentlemen,
the man who now stands
before you, charged with a
breach of the peace, owns
the name of Curran and is
no less a personage than a
relative of the immortal
John Philpot Curran, the
Irish patriot! ” There was
an instant verdict of
acquittal. Every man on
the jury was an O’Hagan
or a Mulcahy !

home habits differ little
from those of, the ordinary
Queensland decently - bred
squatter. He is hot extrava-
gant, but keeps a very good j
table. Each man sitting at
his meals has a clean, ser-’
viette put before him a
luxury which cannot always
be had in the bush. The
only singular feature about
the Tysonian repast is
that, instead of thfe ordin-
ary tea-pot, a, well-seasoned;
black billy - can is placed,
on the table and the tea
is ladled out from it, with a
pint-pot.

Apropos a variety of things, The Bulletin.
asserts that if Kyrle Bellew’s name isn’t, Higgins
it ought to be Higgins. His father, Rev, J, C.-
Bellew, was the only child of Capt. Robert
Higgins, of Lancaster, who married Anna Maria
Montesquieu Bellew, of, “the county Galway,”
bedad. On attaining his majority, agreeable to
the wishes of his friends, he assumed his mother’s
maiden name. In a word, Kyrle Bellew is the
son of arpap whose born name was Higgins.

point, was his ignorance. He probably knew less
and knew it wprfse than,almost, any other, man ip
Melbourne. .Sometime in the ancient long ago he
stood for a place in, the Legislative Council, and
being asked by a constituent if he approved of
trunk lines He is alleged to hav.e replied, after com-
siderable thought, that he did ; in his opinion,
every man should have room to carry his trunk
with him in the train. The constituency evidently
approved of the idea, and Benjy gpt into Parlia-
ment straight, off.

personal. finance, the Parkes «f South Australia.
Ebenezer, an, Adelaide paper relates, got posses-

one of, my tenants !

An,officer of one of H, B. M. ’s ships, now
lysing at Sydney, relates that some years ago when
out on the Swahili Goast be helped to constitute
ap impromptu board of examiners,, which sat to
enquire into the qualifications of a big nigger who
wanted tp become a Govt» interpreter. There is
only png book which has been translated from the
English into the Swahili language,, and that is
“ Atsop’s Tables,” which a former bishop wrote up
into nigger chatter.. The chaplain, who, with the
Captain, completed the board, handed the book
to the son'of Ham, open at the fable of the fox
and the grapes. The candidate, whose English
had been,fpr the most part picked up in the fore-
castle, attached the story valiantly as follows
“Bast commission labbit (rabbit) him see um
bpnch of glapes; him makee jomp ; him no catch;
him, makee jomp again, him no catch ; him makee
jomp one time more; him no catch. Last of all
him say, ‘No (blanky) good’”—which bold
translation pulled the blackfellow through.

In the hierarchy of the affections, women
place love before friendshipmen place friendship
before love. Alphonse Karr told of a man who,
on being refused by a lady who offered to “ remain
his friend,” replied :■ “Thanks, madam. I lova
you. 1 desire to marry you, but my friend —no.
Friendship implies knowledge, respect, congeniality
of tastes. I don't know you well enough to be a
friend of yours.”
Thus a correspondent;—
Etear Bulletin,—l have read with much interest that
the West Australian Land Co., of London, lately declared
al5 per cent, dividend. The interest is purely a selfish
one ; I liold a few shares and trust to sret more shortly,
when I. expect to hear of a dividend of 1500 per cent.—for
!■ know something;. This co. owns, amori}; other proper-
ties, 200 square miles of land beginning 45 miles from
Southern Cross, B. by S. In ’B5, self and mate prospected
this country and found several things worth looking after,
though want of capital and “interest” prevented our
getting them. There is not only gold there but also nit
rates and coal, not to mention other valuable products;
One day we came upon what looked like sponges sticking
out of the ground. We put down a shaft about 80 or 98'
feet and came upon a bed of mineral sponges, exactly
similar in all respects to the ordinary marine sponge
of commerce. 1 have still one I use dailj-, and though
in constant wear for seven years it is as- good as*
new. (Not a patent-medicine “ ad.,” so please read on.)
Well, we got out two or three tons of these sponges and
were reckoning that we could sell them profitably at from
£3 to £(> a ton, when one night it came on to rain—
tropically. We had been smoking and listening to it for
about an hour when Tom asked “ What about those-
sponges?” I replied that L didn’t think the sponges
would come to any harm. “Yes, but we were going to*
get some away to morrow, and now they’ll weigh about a'
ton apiece.” (They averaged three or four feet in-
diameter, roughly.) “Well, we’ll have to wait l till they
dry, that’s all.” But an hour after we found ourselves,-,
tent, ground, and all, slowly rising heavenwards. Haih
an hour later we had risen fully 300 feet. “ flood-
Heavens! where’s this going to stop?-” “Don't know,
and don’t care ; we’re going up, so that’s all right,” II
answered. “ Yes, but we’ve got to come down again..
We must he over a. volcano or .something,” “It’s
all right, Tom, the water’s got down the shaft,,
that’s all, and the sponge-mine is swelling and of course
something’s got to give. It’ll go down again when they
get dry.” And so, of. course, it- did. No doubt the'
sponge-mine, and-the shaft, and the sponges", are there
still, but when the light'eame, in the morning, we found'
ourselves camped half-way up a spur of a range of hills;
which certainly weren’t there the day before, and about-
-20 miles east of; where we ought to have been;: and 1
though we spent three days-trying to find some trace of
that shaft and those sponges we failed utterly. That’s
why I want a mate to go with me, and prospect that
locality some more. Tom won’t go, he’s superstitious;;
and besides, he’s made a pile since then. He made his-
pile out of another discovery in the same neighbourhood,,
flie particulars of which are too astonishing to mention.
Yours &c., P.'C.J.

Betitled and belittled ; there isn’t much

The Broken Hill strike caused a decrease,
to October 8, of <£109,413 in S.A. railway revenue.
Parkes is becoming solicitous for the
Unemployed. He is unemployed himself just now.
Why doesn’t some one prosecute a parson
for having his place of business open on a Sunday?
The Shiels Government is essentially a
protective one. It is even protecting M. H.
Davies.
James Service says there are at present a
thousand ex-convicts from New Caledonia in the
Australias.
The Earl of Onslow thinks Maoriland a
splendid place to send Salvation colonies. Re
venge is sweet.
A Compulsory Arbitration Bill is now
being discussed by the French Parliament. And
introduced by the French Premier, too.
The Saltation Army’s finances are in a
straitened condition. The finger of Providence is
here; they will not now trouble us with those
“settlements.”
The last expiring act of the Holder
Ministry (S.A.) was to gazette 40 new J.’sP.
This makes 1600 magistrates for 320,000 people, or
an average of 1 in 200.
N.S.W, now owes £*55,000,000. The
public works of the province pay the interest on
this less 1 per cent., which has to be made good
out of the ordinary revenue.
The kernel of Queensland’s 50 per cent,
reduction of payment to Ms.P. is, of course, the
desire to keep out the poor politician and let in
the one with an axe to grind.

pression of Vice Bill is a direct attack upon

Christian Churches.— Dipso.

of-confidence division, the Dibbs Government has
been occupied in studying political meteorology
and carrying its umbrella accordingly.

Q.C., from the attack of Attorney-General Stock,
in the S.A. Assembly, the other day, Mrs.

loudly.

privately urge Grainger, M.P., to press on with

brewers against it.

members of the Playford crowd which their pre-
sent chief, Downer, assisted to kick out. This
illustrates better than anything the present condi-
tion of S.A. politics.
Ninian Melville, who as cabinet-maker
made the chair he sits in as N.S.W. Chairman of
Committees, has recently elevated it and can now
see over the top of the despatch-boxes that line
the table in front of him.

opposes payment of members, always sends his

Premier Shiels had a phenomenally fine
opportunity to make a hit in the character of Pure
White Patriot, but wasn’t white enough to seize it.
The uninvolved husband of a rich wife, Shiels
could have afforded to cut all connection with the
cronk crowd who held him back, and pour legal
fire and brimstone upon Gomorrah from the
pinnacle of Fame. He might have taken all the
credit for ordering the inevitable, instead of trying
to dodge it and pretending that it hadn’t come.
Few young men get such chances to advertise
their political honesty. Providence must be
heartily disgusted at Shiels’ want of foresight.

A DREAM OF THE ARGENTINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
well, it s good-bye, lads, for the skipper hails—
We are going across the brine,
And the wind is filling the good ship’s sails
That is bound for the Argentine,
For the verdant plains in the Sunny South,
For the land of corn and wine.
And the North will mourn for the sturdy sons
Who have left her in her need,
The prey of the land-grab syndicate
And the sharks of human breed,
And of them who hold that the slaving-trade
Bits in with the Christian creed.
For the slaver sails to the Southern seas,
And is blessed by the pious band,
While the cheap Hindoo and the vile Chinese
Pour in Crowds on every hand,
And a false and corrupted Government
Is blighting all the land.
For the sturdy white they count too dear,
And they plot his overthrow’;
Iff we fly the field then their plot succeeds
And as slaves our brethren go—
Shall we then like cow-ards shun the fight?
We awake and we thunder “ NO ! ”
The skipper stands on his deck and hails,
And his ship is on the brine,
But in vain the land-breeze fills his sails—
We are not for the Argentine,
For it’s shoulder to shoulder we’re standing now,
And we will not break our line.
R.S.
“Opals and Agates," or "Bartley on
Woman."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If, when in Brisbane, you should see a
carelessly - dressed, face - furrowed, skin - briek-
dusted, lank, kindly-looking old gentleman,
huddled well forward on an ancient grey equine
of drooping style and unpretentious shambling—
the said old gentleman taking no notice of any
person whatsoever, but ambling steadily onwards,
with a loose bridle-rein, legs long in the stirrups,
and a general air of gone-pastness-that old gen-
tleman is Nehemiah Bartley, who has just written
a work under the title “Opals and Agates; or,
Scenes under the Southern Cross and the Magel-
hans ; being Memories of Fifty Years of Australia
and Polynesia.”
• * •
“ Opals and Agates” is, as a title, unique
as the author. It is positively mystifying, until
you find that the gems are dedicated to “ the Girl
Patriots, past and present, of Australasia,” and
you discern at once the poetical compliment paid
to the sex by this preax chevalier. Mr. Bartley
has a zest for lovely woman and springing girl-
hood of all kinds whatsoever, and he choruses the
fact in the beginning, through the middle, and at
the end of the book with a candour that does
credit _to his heart and a catholicity of taste
that is true religion. It is the dominant
note, in fact, of all the author’s side-reflections. In
Graudisbnian language he describes his wanderings
and adventures since ’49, when he entered Aus-
tralasia by way of Hobart, Tas. Generally enter-
taining, profusely anecdotical, replete with quaint
“tips,’’ the book is yet too “diary-ish.” The
author’s confession that it was drawn from a diary
kept for 50 years was quite unnecessary.
• » «
It takes a knotty-minded man to keep a
diary “for long,” and Mr. Bartley, judged by
“Opals and Agates,” is a poetical statistician (are
Coghlan and Hayter listening ?) who, in the
midst of a Bulwerish rhapsody, parenthesises the
exact height to a foot of a mountain (and, by the
way, the exact height of a girl!)—an author
armed with an aneroid and a Tasso, a pick and a
Euclid, a bullock-yoke and a Venus (many of
Her !) The intention of the book seems to be to
contrast modes Australian with vogues English.
Although Mr. Bartley keeps obtruding the fact
that he is English and loyal, yet he generally
awards the palm to Australia. There is no plot
to the work, unless it be to screech out the moral
at all points that our author, though emphatically
a Cockney, admires woman in all her variegations.
In short, the book’s title might less inscrutably
have been “ Bartley on Woman.”
* * »
The Bartleian philosophy seems to be that
a man should love extensively and well. After
stating that statistics tell that there are “on
hand ” at any moment “at least five millions of
beautiful marriageable girls,” and that it would
take any young man 30 years to superintend a
march-past of the lot
Yet, each individual Damon and Chloe elects to go mad
over some one “ bright particular star ” of the other sex,
and he, or she, for the lime being, totally ignores the
claims to admiration of the remaining 4,999,999 can-
didates.
And listen s
A foot is as pretty, in its way, as a face, and possesses
the advantage that it can hear to be stared at, without
growing embarrassed, as the face does, at times; and it,
at all events, is quite Unconscious of its beauty, which is
more than the face always is ; playful, uninteliectua!, and
charming in its restless, unaccountable, floor-tapping
ways, the female foot is, most appropriately, covered with
the skin of that most erratic and playful of all animals,
the kid ; and, in a thoroughbred woman, it is shapely and
imaged at 60, as at 16; hut the same can never he said of
the face, even of the most high-born.
Once more;
Lucy weighed 9st. and was sft. Sin. Laura was 10st.,
sft. sin., and each of them was seven times as long as her
foot, a proof that they were well-proportioned.
* # #
Mr, Bartley’s “ best girl ” is the Tahitian
and consequently he doesn’t describe her too
freely, but of the nude Samoan girls, lie remarks
that “ their magnificent physical health was
attested by their limts and bodies, which, though
they were symmetry itself, were as firm and
elastic, as if carved from cork, or indiarubber.” ! !
From whence he draws a moral against the less
healthy white woman, thus emphasising certain
pro-Kanaka-slavery views enunciated by him in
his preface.
“But the Tahiti and Eimeo girls wer#
admitted to be ‘something more exquisite still.”
From which one deduces the conclusion that Eng
land ought to buy the Society Islands from
France I Now, isn’t this “ rather?”
* * *
Our author never misses any chance oj
mentioning a pretty girl. The wife of a Carolina
Island trader struck his envious fancy thus
“With long eye-lashes, voluptuous form, black
eyes, full of sub-latent amber fire (I never saw
such glorious eyes), and feet barely a span long, a
c °y> jolly girl, a veritable ‘Maitai whahini,’was
our Yankee friend’s Sultana, and, sweet sixteen,
(sly old dog, Lewis).”
* * #
But, there ! it’s woven right through the
carpet, and to be found everywhere in Bartley,
who actually rhapsodises over two Murrumbidgee
gins! “For curly hair, brilliant black eyes, and
pure white teeth, it were hard to beat them.”
Brunton Stephens must write another “Ode.”
But, say, Mr. Bartley, are you really married ?
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
A Melbourne barrister says that the proper
tribunal to decide the question of sanity or in-
sanity of a murderer is the jury who sit upon his
body after execution.
It is related that at a fashionable ball a
young naval officer had been enjoying himself
at supper, and later on found himself tete-a-
tete in an ante-room with the raseinating
younpp wife of a leading citizen, to whom
he _ was making desperate love. His arms
encircled her waist, when she espied her lawful
spouse, evidently in search of her, rapidly ap-
proaching. “Let me go, quick!” cried she, en-
deavouring to disengage herself from the young
fellow. “ What’s your (hie) hurry ?” “My
husband’s coming, and if he sees you he’ll knock
you down.’’ “Then I’m on the ho—horns of a
dil —dilemma. If 1 hold on I shall be knocked
down ; if I let go I shall fall down. I’ll risk it,
and hold on.” And he did—the anxious husband
passing out of sight.
Alex. Brown, M.L.C. (N.S.W.) on the
advantages of flogging :
Half a dozen or a dozen strokes of the lash promptly
and pertinently administered as soon as that decision was
given, and then the lad thrown out amongst his comrades,
that would soon deal with the difficulty.
Also, on the same basis, if a convicted murderer
were promptly hanged, say about ten minutes
after sentence is passed—the hangman could be
waiting beside the dock and the knot could be
fixed under defendant’s ear while the jury was
consulting—and then his dead body were thrown
through the gaol gate into the crowd, that would
soon deal with the other difficulty. Only there is
one thing which wise Brown has forgotten : the
Government should keep the partially-flayed
prisoner's clothes and bounce him out through the
front gate with nothing on but a towel. If the
warning that is writ large on his back is calculated
to do so much good, then it is simply idiotic to let
the great moral lesson be hidden where no one can
see it.
Dear Bulletin, —We had hauled out into
the stream at Newcastle, N.S.W.; the trim-
mers had all left; we were to sail in the morn-
ing. The skipper, a pusillanimous mean-spirited
Dutchman, had broken his promise to allow us to
spend our last night in port ashore. Growls,
threats, and curses filled the fo’e’s’l. Dave, a
hard case, swore that before the voyage was
out he would drop a pulley or a spike from aloft
on the skipper. Harry Finch, the smartest,
merriest fellow aboard, had promised to see Big
Nell (a voluptuous harpy who had got through his
12 months’pay) that evening. “Boys,” said he,
“ I am going to swum it. I must see Nell.
I’ll bring some beer with me when I am coming
back. Keep a good look out, if you hear my hail.”
In vain we tried to dissuade him. A strong
ebb—three knots an hour—was rushing out,
and it was a good half-mile athwart
the current to the wharf. Over the bows he went,
hat, boots, and all, and, with a cheery “ I’m all
right, maties, ” he struck out for the wharf. He
never reached it. Led by a base passion for a
baser woman, he met his fate—a modern Leander
swimming a muddy Hellespont to court the
caresses of an impure Hero. Next day the usual
stolid jury returned a verdict that the deceased,
H. Finch, a hard drinker, had met his death by
drowning in attempting to swim ashore to get a
pint cf colonial beer. —D.
Mr. J. C. Neild, M.P., the distinguished
author of the “ Bill to make people virtuous by
Act of Parliament ” which he calls for short the
“Bill for the Suppression of Vice,” will be in-
terested. as will also b<3 the Sydney Social Purity
Society, in the news that certain ladies of Berlin
have formed themselves into a secret society for
the purpose of watching their husbands' and
punishing unfaithful ones. There are said to be
already 200 members of this dread order, all be-
longing to the higher official and military classes.
They have organised a detective service, and join
personally in the pleasures of the chase. The
Sydney Social Purity Society will learn with com-
placency that one of their own most approved
methods has been adopted by this formidable
body. One lady disguised herself as a needle-
woman, hired a room next to one to which the
suspect was known to resort, and bored a hole
through the partition. This augurs ill for hus-
bands, and is likely to lead to an aggravation of
the common disfigurement of wives’ eyes, inflamed
by weeping and watching. The name of this dark
and dismal society, unillumined by a ray of light,
save such as may stream through key-holes and
gimlet-holes, is the Gchenne Frauenbund. A
superficial acquaintance with high Dutch enables
us to interpret Frauenbund as Women’s League,
but Gehenne is a matter of speculation. Possibly
it signifies a Gehenna of a Women’s-League.

The officers and crew of H.M.S. Rapid seems to have had a most
delightful cruise lately.
“The ship left Sydney on May 18, called at Queensland ports, and
then made for the Solomon Islands. Samarai, New Guinea, was touched
at en route. Upon arrival in the Solomons, matters were found-to be quiet
generally. Efforts were made to capture several murderers in the group,
but without success, the culprits making further inland, and so eluding
their pursuers. Punishment was, however, inflicted by the Rapid’s people
destroying the villages and plantations of the suspected murderers. Alto-
gether seven villages were destroyed. Some very good curios, were obtained
while the ship was at the islands.” —Daily Paper.

Society
The drollest sight to be seen in Melbourne
is a certain fuddled Crtesus trying to escape the
toils of some sharps who want to lure him on to
hazard, or “poker,” or any other sport at which
he can be relieved of his spare cheques. At these
times the poor man has a tolerably well-defined
idea that he’ll be safe if he can only get home, to
which end he hails a cab. Once aboard the cab
all is well with Joseph, but it has sometimes hap-
pened that the other gentlemen grabbed at him
like so many Potiphar’s wives, and managed to
pull him back by the coat-tails before he got
fairly seated. Then the fine hit bird bad to moult
a feather or two, we suppose.
• # #
The last (and first) bank directors prose-
cuted in Victoria for malversation, were those of
the_ Provincial and Suburban Bank. That insti-
tution was started in Smith-st., Fitzroy, about 20
years ago, as an accommodation for small trades-
men, but it lent money to a big railway contractor
who failed, and the bank came to grief and
liquidation. Amongst the directors was an ex-
Mayor of Collingwood. One of the losers and
Crown witnesses was W. E, Adcock, who has been
in Adelaide gaol for the last four years for “con-
tempt of court.” Chief Justice Stawell presided
at the trial, 12 years ago, and only fined such of
the directors as were found guilty.
A man now in Sydney is biting his lip
hard. Some months ago, in England, his feelings
were carried away by a photo, and letters from his
cousin in the Australian bush. The photo, showed
the cousin on a fine horse, dressed in riding-
breeches, top-boots and cabbage-tree hat, and the
letters told of nuggets of gold, and a glorious life ;
of shooting, and riding, also of wattle, jackasses,
stockwhips, and of sundry mates (mostly Jims).
So he (the recipient) gave up a decent position as
the world now goes and took passage for Australia
only to find his hero cousin installed as general
pipper in a bush pub. with a Chinese cook kicking
him about. He had enough cash left to get back
to Sydney, and is now looking for any employ-
ment at any price. Where the cousin borrowed
the horse from he doesn’t know.
# # *
The latest news concerning the “ New
Australia ” movement (for the emigration of 10,000
Australian bush-workers to South America) is that
the coming Paradise is to be situated on the banks
of the Rio Negro, on the Patagonian border-line,
where the overgrown aborigine can conveniently
howl with his awful voice across the river at the
settlers and otherwise make himself agreeable. The
Negro is a treacherous river with a bar at the mouth
which effectually blocks commerce, and the locality
is used as a penal settlement, chiefly for niggers
and other persons of no account. It is reputed to
be a barren and stony region, haunted by cold
winds, and afflicted with much snow and slush, so
that Don Lane will have to keep his sombrero
dragged well down over his ears in the winter to
protect him from the inclemency of the weather,
and Don Gilberto Casey will be well-advised to
lay in a good supply of thick boots and take his
mackinproof along with him. Also, both these
gentlemen would do well to read up the Spanish
language, and study the manners and customs of
the people among whom they propose to settle. A
high-toned and lofty courtesy is their strong
point. The individual who possesses even a t inge
of Spanish blood will bow eight times on an
average while asking for a light for his cigar, and
the other man will bow eight times while giving
it. They both bow to begin with; then the man
who has the light blows the ash of his own cigar
and hands it over with a bow, and the other
fellow bows, and lights up with it; next, he
returns it with a bow, and the first man
bows also, and, after that, they both bow
promiscuously, and take off their hats several
times, and express a hope that both of them will
live 1000 years. If Don Smith or any other of
the emigrants asks a hidalgo for a gory match, or
if he is asked for a light and responds in Banana-
land fashion by igniting a Incifer on the hinder
regions of his ignoble carcase, there will be trouble
in Argentina. The man who wants to prosper in
South America must be polite. The native in-
variably is ; even if he has choked his wife into a
state, of insensibility he always bows to the servant
while asking her for a jug of water to throw over
the insensible lady and bring her round. Also,
he bows to the lady herself when she comes round.
The Australian doesn’t. He simply leaves her on
the floor.
# * *
The Bulletin would also strongly urge
the “New Australia” people to learn how
to assume a respectful attitude when they see
a priest, or even ten priests. Further, they should
go well supplied with insect-powder. The Pata-
gonian, who will probably come over to see them
when they get settled, wants it badly. No one
knows how this is for he bathes pretty regularly—
he and all the women of his settlement bathe
together, in fact and his habits in this
direction may lead him to get into the local
water-tank. In such a case the best thing
to do is to bow to him and ask him
to do himself the honour to get out,
and then bnv to him again when he is out.

Don Lane and his colleagues ought also to prac*
tise wearing the Spanish cloak, which is a graceful
garment so long as the wearer knows how to cast
the tail of it over his shoulder instead of letting it
trail between his legs in the slush. If they can
take with them a remedy for white ants and
locusts it will be advisable. There are billions of
these insects, and the process of smashing them
with a club on a board has proved altogether
ineffectual. Also, they are urgently advised not
to mash the Argentine maidens too recklessly.
The really high-toned thing to do in these parts
when a man is mashed on a strange girl is to dis-
course sweet music outside her window at night,
but if Dun Lane takes his concertina and sings,
in a hoarse voice, “ We’ve worked eight hours
to day, ” the chances are that the girl may tell her
father, and the old man may come out and bow
ten times to the visitor, and then empty a
Winchester into him.
It is now asserted that Matthew Davies
(knight) has been retaining the services of Purves,
Q.C., since 1887, of Dr. Madden, Topp and
Higgins, since 1890; and of Isaacs, since the
Mercantile Bank collapsed. If these retainers
mean that none of the counsel aforementioned can
appear against Davies it is strange that lie didn’t
engage the whole Bar at once. But there must be
some mistake here. Purves recently told the
Victorian Natives that the Government’s indis-
position to prosecute certain bank directors was
ruining the credit of Victoria. He surely can’t
intend to go back upon that statement with an
impassioned speech to the effect that the prosecu-
tion of Davies is a burning shame !

The trunks of a new chum were lately
detained in a Wellington (M.L.) hotel as security
for the high-collared one’s unpaid bill. When
they were opened, after many days, they proved
to be full of beads, penny dolls, and brass
jewellery, which he had brought out from ’Ome to
exchange with the natives for land. The “native,”
in the Briton's vocabulary, is the same naked and
simple ass to the end of time, and whenever Bull
hears of an aboriginal anywhere he at once decides
that a penny doll and a brass toe ring are the
things which that aboriginal will swap his soul for.
The versatile “Pr fessur ”
Clampett, while travelling Tas-
mania as a vocalist and pug.,
one night rested at the “ camp ”
kept by one Webb, a noted
joker. The professor’s reputa-
tion as a vocalist having reached
the camp, Webb invited the
nearest road-gang to drop in and
feast their ears on the warbling
of the “ prof.” In the course of
the evening, Webb asked the
guest to sing some songs in the
bar “ among the lads, who never
hear anything up here, you
know.” Clampett sang accord-
ingly. Next morning the de-
parting songster asked for his
bill, which was promptly ren-
dered. A few moments after-
wards the professor handed the
bill back to the astounded
humorist, endorsed ; —“To sing-
ing two songs and entertaining
visitors, 15s. (Webb’s bill was
the same amount.) Reed, set-
tlement by accommodation.”
Webb tried to laugh it off, but
the professor was adamant, and
left at once by the coach.

The Sydney and Melbourne newspaper
press is now being flooded with boy reporters. In
thq “good old days” there was generally one of
these youngsters on each paper, and he was called
“ the junior.” His office was to “do” the subur-
ban courts and be fagged by the seniors. Now,
these sucking geniuses are called by stingy pro-
prietors and managers “cadets,” and instead of
being paid ill a week, the offices may actually get
a premium with them. A boy’s parents might as
well pay Satan to admit him to Sheol.

Six men were frost-bitten at Newcastle,
N.S.W., last week—while stowing mutton in the
hold of the Duke of Westminster, at a tempera-
ture eight degrees below zero. One had his nose
bitten ; the other five, their hands. Despite this
danger and the extra inconvenience of working in
an Arctic atmosphere, the mutton-stowers receive
only a shilling-an-bour. In previous years they
were paid eighteenpence-an-hour, yet, when com-
bined capital, without warning, decided to reduce
the wage, not the faintest opposition was made
by the Union. The Newcastle Wharf Labourers
Union, however, only includes about one-third of
the total number of labourers ; the others, mostly
called Hans or Antonio, do not believe in the
“ Junion hence its impotence.

The best up to date :
Pope.— On the 30th September last, at her late resi-
dence, Toorak, Mrs. Harriet Pope, relict of the late
George Pope, England, and daughter of Peter Shaw
Luard, son of Francis l.uard, solicitor, of Chancery-lane,
London ; niece of Colonel Luard, who rendered such dis-
tinguished service in the Peninsular war as to earn the
Peninsular medal, afterwards as adjutant of the 16th
Light Dragoons at Waterloo, where his horse was shot
from under him. In 1834 he obtained a majority, and
served in the staff of Sir Charles Dalbiac, his uncle. On
the Queen’s coronation he was made Brevet Lieutenant-
Colonel, and in the same year he returned to India as
military secretary to Sir Jasper Nicolls, Commander-in-
Chief. On her mother’s side Mrs. Pope was also niece of
the Uev. Dr. Summerville, of Edinburgh, Scotland, and
closely related to Professor Davy, the eminent lecturer on
phrenology ; cousin also of Dr. Tom Davy, London, who
married the niece of Earl Bathurst. Mrs. Pope leaves an
only surviving sister in the person of Mrs. Emma Dunn,
known as a very old and respected resident of Castle-
maine, but now residing with her family at Gourlay*
street, Balaclava.

* * #
The head-master of a certain Sydney State
school lately asked his department for a female
assistant teacher. His wife also applied, thus—
“ Please send the plainest girl you have to my
husband.” It is not uncommon for a teacher’s
wife to interview the heads of the department,
and to request them to substitute plain female
assistants for those already at her husband’s
school.

A domestic left the house of a prominent
Northern white Civil Servant to enter the
maternity ward of the Salvation Army. The
lasses pressed for the name of the author of the
mischief. The girl gave the name of the official,
her master, and he was duly approached for a
subscription. He sent £2 25., but as this was not
thought enough he was again canvassed, and
benevolently subscribed a further <£s ss. When
the little stranger made its appearance it was a
black child, and everybody—so depraved is
modern society—is surprised. That official’s
coachman is as black as night. Kind actions are
often misinterpreted.
* * #
The Sydney Women’s Literary Association
has sent an address of condolence to Lady Tenny-
son. The literary ladies with the undarned stock-
ings feel that they and Tennyson were in the
same line of business, and they mourn for their
fellow litterateur, just as the Chinaman with the
vegetable-basket laments over the failure of the
new loan, and says that what this country wants
is more confidence in commercial circles.
,* # *
The disagreements of Australian juries in
finance cases are becoming monotonous. They
must be attributable either to bewildering sum-
mings-up by the Judge, or to some mysterious
power behind the jury-box.

A Gippsland bush pub. Time, night.
Scene, tlie “ best ” room in the house. Daughter
of the landlord tum-tumming and screeching at
the piano. Audience, her adorer —a weak-chested
bantam with cash. The twain are disturbed by
the entrance of a stock-rider just about half-
loaded. “Ah,” (/wc) says he, “me name’s
Maguire. Oi’ve a brother a doctor in Queensland,
another a squatter, any amount of money, money
is a mere trifle, and I’m alone in this act j but, by
Jove, Oi’d like to hear the young lady sing”—
(hie). The young lady (experienced in'the ways
of “drunks”) tum-tummed away on the music-
box, never troubling to look around at the in-
truder. Not so her adorer. He took the floor and
in tones of subdued thunder addressed the stock-
rider : “Excuse me, sir, you are intruding here,
Josephus Clarke is looking had,
His tints are red and yellow ;
That Clara P. will drive him mad,
She will indeed, poor fellow 1
A mention of the frisky dame
Makes Joey shake and shiver,
And when he dies they’ll find her name
Endorsed upon his liver.
He either backed her little bill
Or told a little story ;
He’ll give the riddle up until
He flutters off to glory ;
Then, should St. Peter tackle Joe,
The simple truth inviting,
He'll answer, “ How am I to know?
It wasn’t like my writing.”
And Clara, when St. Peter brings
The paper to the matron,
Will shake her head and flap her wings,
And nudge her former patron.
“ Who hacked that bill ? Upon the scene
The Law has dropped a curtain ;
’Twas I, or Joe it may have been —
We never knew for certain.”

A lurid lime-light on German sausage. A
butcher belonging to South Melbourne was
charged recently with having on his premises the
black, damp, flabby carcase of a bullock which
had evidently died from old age and long-standing
disease. The flesh-dealer s defence was that it
was absolutely necessary to have such horrors on
hand because they made the best German
sausage.” He was fined all the same, and the
Bench abjured sausage right there. A total
sausage abstinence society is a thing that seems to
be much wanted in Australia.

Dear Bulletin,-—Your correspondent,
“ Crow-eater,” who writes re Adelaide Botanical
Gardens, has much regard for the late Dr. Schoiti-
burgk, but very little knowledge of the Gardens.
They never contained an avenue of gums, either
stately or otherwise, so it is needless to say that
nobody rooted it up. Fifteen years ago there
were more flowers in the gardens than there are
now, but that only proves my contention that Dr;
Schomburgk’s neglect in the last decade of his life
had let a paradise degenerate into a tangled
Wilderness. Palm-house, conservatories, fourt-
tains, and Victoria Regia house were all in shock-
ing dis repair, while the herbarium was also up-
side down when the present director took charge;
' ours, X.
A certain highly respect-
able temperance restaurant
of Sydney one that for-
bids its waitresses flirting
on pain of instant dismissal
—supplies the following :
An ordinarily good-looking
waitress receives on an aver-
age three invitations per
day for the theatre; six in-
vitations to come for a drive
or a walk in the evening.
The restaurant is open on
Sunday for tea, and on that
evening the average number
of offers “ to wait outside ”
is 15. Invitations for the
theatre come almost wholly
from young bachelors; re-
quests to “meet me alone”
come in nine cases out of
ten from elderly men.
# # *
It is rumoured that at
least one member of a Syd-
ney family of good social
position has developed
leprosy, and it is certain
that the inmates, white and
coloured, of the Little Bay
leper-station (an institution
described some months ago
in a Bulletin article which
has set the Sydney Sunday
papers writing nothing but
leper “copy ”) are increasing
rapidly. It is also certain
that the N. S. W. health
officials have under obser-
vation as suspected lepers
more whites than they like
to admit. It is likewise a
fact that the Kanaka-labour
curse is extending to the
N. S. W. sugar - plantations.
When Federal Australia has
its Molokai with 1000 doomed
inmates it will be able to see
the price it pays for consort-
ing with the “cheap” Orien-
tal races and the .£6-a-year
black trash from the South
Seas.
The other day a man at North Sydney?
who feared to punish his troublesome horse while
mounted, tied it up to a lamp-post and threw
blue-metal at it from a distance of about 20 paces.
Noble animal!
* 9 *
A man who broke his leg in a paddock
near Hawera (M.L.), writes to* the papers to say
that he shouted “ help ” for four hours and 1 nobody
came near him, but when he started to yell
“ murder,” instead, about six people were on the
spot in ten minutes. Nobody wants to help any-
body, but everybody wants to see a murder.

A Sydney architect recently advertised for
a draftsman. One of the unsuccessful appli-
cations :
Sir,—ln answer to your advt. I beg to offer my services
as a distinct profession in the interpretation of the works
of others, with intelligence and aptitude for this parti-
cular work. In this specialism of skill entrusted to me it
will be done minutely, and full analysis, and, I venture to
hope, with integrity, impartiality, just performance in
the spirit of the basis of the contract, which is tenable, to
be regarded with confidence for upright dealing, recti-
tude, and ability ; whose function is to gain a consensus
of opinion, an arbiter who has a knowledge of the work.
In soliciting your consideration, I trust, and hope, to
iscure your approval.—Yours, &0., ——.
At a recent very select Melbourne chavoo
afc the expense of Tom, Dick, and Harry
His Excellency the Governor responded to the toast of
his health, proposed in eulogistic terms by the chairman.
Lord Hopetoun said Victoria was apparently satisfied at
having taken i the “ loyal side ” some ye, ars ago en the
question of the selection of Governors and expressed a
preference for the appointment of Her Majesty’s repre-
sentatives by Her Majesty and Her advisers rather than
Lave the Governor elected So long as Victoria consented
« receive a Governor so chosen, he (Lord Hopetoun) was
confident the most desirable man would be selected.
Bachelor Baronet Ogle has been appointed
commander of H.M.S. Rapid on the Australian
station. Joy !if he only acts up to his name.

THE DAILY JANUS.
GOOD OLD MAN (from the country) : “Give me an evening paper,
my boy.”
BOY: “Yes, sir. Here you are, sir—all the winners."
G.O.M. (testily) : “Haven’t you a paper without any racing news
in it?”
BOY: “No, sir. There’s the EKKER, it has the racin' on one
side, but it has a sermon on the other.”
(EKKER accepted.)

# # •
The Governor of New Caledonia leaves
next month for France. The reasons for his conge
are unknown, as he is most popular at Noumea.
# * *
One day, last week, a book-vendor sold 37
copies of Zola’s “Nana ” in Sydney Lands office.
* # #
Judge Boucaut (of S.A., who is on his
travels): “ The Scotch accent of a pretty little
girl is charming.” Certainly. And the Irish
accent of a pretty little girl is most fascinating.
And the Welsh accent of a pretty little girl is
simply enchanting. But what of the ugly big
girl, O Boucaut, the not-blind ?

The Chicago' (U.S.A.) Fair has been’
“dedicated” by the clergy. Dedicated to what?
Wooden nutmegs ?
* * *
The first Sydney divorce-case this term
was —Love v. Love. A misprint, we presume, for
Love v. Marriage.
* * *
Twenty-five thousand 1 golden sovereigns
followed the late Way Key to China. Sydney
could spare the corpse, but not the corpse’s
tailings.
An effective way of knocking the poetry
out of a spoony couple is that lately practised by
certain misguided Sydney youths who run a brush
with some sticky mixture thereon along all the
park seats where lovers are wont to sit and cuddle
of an evening.
* # #
The Eton jackets now so fashionable with
ladies are vulgarly known as “freezers.”
* * #
It is a not uncommon practice among
certain leading Sydney houses to commission
the auctioneer conducting the sale of some “ big ”
man’s household goods to add to the list part of
their stock, somewhat soiled for the occasion. A
smart Hebrew broker some time ago turned up at
one establishment with articles he had just bought
to have them cleaned up, and learned that he had
paid <£3o for what he might have bought there
for £26.
* * *
The masher collar was invented by a mail
Who attempted suicide by cutting his throat. He
adopted the stiff, starched stand-up to hide the
Scar that encircled his neck.
# * *
A certain Anglican parson employed at
a big Southern cemetery recently represented to
the authorities that all the Protestafit pauper
O. D.’s (professional term for corpses of “other
denominations ”) are handed over to the Wesleyan
crow, who scoops all the fees appertaining to the
burial thereof, which he urged was wrong, and he
asked for a share. He was referred to the burial
contractor who is supposed to “ sling in ” a clergy-
man with the coffin and other mummeries of
Christian burial.
# • #
Primate Smith writes the Bondi (Sydney)
Anglican congregation that if they insist upon
refusing to accept as their pastor the clergyman
he has been pleased to send them, they will be
“striving against the will of God.” Surely the
Deity’s name isn’t Smith !
m m 9
It is currently reported that, during the
summer months, the elite of Darlinghurst prison
will only play lawn-tennis in the afternoon, after
hard labour, commencing play at five o’clock.
* # #
S.A. police are to have bicycles instead of
horses. Erom observations on the spot, we are
led to believe that go-carts would better suit the
situation.
# # #
A deputation of girls waited on Minister
of Defence Davis (Vic.), complaining that they
had been thrown out of work by the closing of the
local ammunition-factory. The Minister was
unctuously sympathetic, but deplored the fact
that the girls preferred this work in town to the
healthy surroundings of the country, and advised
them to leave the factories and take domestic ser-
vice. “Become servants!” was the answer. “No
fear. We wouldn’t be our own missuses then and
knockoff at fixed hours.” “Eight hours” is a
man’s day’s work. Who will blame girls for ob-
jecting to work sixteen, under the control of a
woman ?
* * *
‘Surely,” wails a Melbourne paper, “it
is a sign of hard times when the burglars enter
the churches in quest of booty?” Not at all.
It’s merely a sign of the spread of intelligent dis-
crimination among the criminal classes.
* # *
The first act of importance on the part of
the initial batch of “ free-labourers,” on arriving
at Broken Hill, was to pick the pockets of the
police engaged guarding them.
* * *
As a result of the Vic. Minister of Defence
having stopped free railway riding to uniformed
militia-men, the tolling, vociferous, uniformed
larrikin is ho longer a special feature of the
Bourke-street crowd on Saturday nights.
The Australasian Institute of Journalists
is now open to women.

“ In Memoriam ” from Melbourne :
Art thou in Heaven, or where can you be?
Father, dear father, we still think of thee.
It was meant in kindness* no doubt, but the family
that feels so doubtful as all that as to the old man’s
hereafter should keep quiet.

“Nine English lobsters have been im-
ported alive by the lonic for the Otago (M.L.)
Acclimatisation Society.” This beastly country
will soon be worth living in.
# # •
A story of exaggerated honesty is told by
Geo. Green Well, of Toora (Vic.). While in busi-
ness at Grassy Gully diggings, he had given credit
to the extent of <£l2 to one Smith. Two years
latet he heard of a corresponding Smith working
irt a claim 15 miles away, and on Tuesday night
he posted him the bill. At 2.30 a.m. on Thursday,
Smith knocked at Greenwell’s door. He said that
his father was the debtor, that the old man had
been drowned at Adelaide a year before, that he
had been until then ignorant of the debt, and that
he had brought the money. That man had
come out with the midnight shi ft, and then walked
15 miles along a bush-track to pay his dead father’s
■debt.
It is on the cards (says our London corres-
pondent) that Phil. Mennell’s last journalistic
venture, the Australian Mail —which has made
good headway—will be amalgamated with the
British Australasian.

A COMING EXPOSITION—“CHICAGO.”
ẅ
Victorians who took the loyal side,
Here’s proof your instincts were both high and
true
In letting gracious Majesty decide
Who was the proper man to govern you.
Ye judged the Right Divine could scarcely fail
To pick of all men born the very pearl,
And ye were right—she sent by early mail
That “ most desirable ” young man, the Earl.
He’s thin and fragile, and he’s “ sickled o’er ”
With pallid casts, not all by vigils wrought.
But simply showing that his azure gore
Don’t circulate as freely as it ought;
His statesmanship’s a quantity unknown,
His speech afflicts the country like a ban.
But ye can rest content for he has shown
He is a most desirable young man.
Ye might have wished for brains, and yearned to
place
A strong mart at the head of your affairs,
To have a man of Austral choice and race
To bear the load of presidential cares,
But bravely drove ye all such treason hence,
And all the nations of the earth agree
That though you’ve lost your claim to common
sense
You have preserved your noble loya \teei
OVER.
Stir not dead ashes, darling, let them lie
(I call you sweet names still, hypocrite I)
There’s not an ember underneath, I know,
Pan till ydu break your heart —there is no flow.
Let them lie still—wise is this wish of mine;
Better a cold, grey memory than no sign ;
Vehement prayers but scatter far and wide
These fluttering ghosts of love that lived—and died.
Fire does not leap, dear, for a fretful word.
Even your kisses leave my pulse unstirred ;
If a dead woman lay there fn cold rest
You were a fool to warm her on your breast.
INA M. W.
