From Walgett (N.S.W ) News :
On last Thursday night a large black snake was killed
by Mr. \V. Hayes. This is early in the year for our reptil-
lian kin to be on the warpath.
Our “ reptillian kin !”
The “ Jerusalem Coffee House,” which in
olden times used to be the great rendezvous for
Australians in London, after many vicissitudes
appears to be in a fair way to become once more a
kind of “Colonial Exchange.” The time-honoured
quarters in Cowper’s Court have been given up,
and the affair has been taken over by a limited
liability co., new premises have been secured in
Billiter-street, E. 0., and there is every probability
of the “New Jerusalem” achieving the success
the promoters anticipate. But merchant princes
no longer meet “ on Change ” to discuss business
and make bargains, and no more melancholy sight
presents itself in London to-day than the Royal
Exchange, the great quadrangle of which is now
deserted by city men and serves merely as a short
cut from Cornhill to Thread needle-street. One by
one the glories of the “ City ” are departing. The
old order changeth and giveth place to the new,
and the famous spot where so many adventures
were plotted, so many voyages of discovery
planned, where the Old East India Company
was first conceived, and where in more recent
times the Rothschilds and other giants of finance
used to meet every day after lunch to discuss the
affairs of the world is now given over to a needy
out-at-elbows lot of people.
Marupa Still Gaining
Ground.
A WELL-KNOWN COMMERCIAL GENTLEMAN
GIVES HIS TESTIMONY.
Birmingham, England,
January 3rd. 1892.
Mr. O. I. Kempthorne.
Dear Sir, —You will remember the bad cold and
tore throat I had when leaving New Zealand for
England. Well, thanks to that “Maori stuff”
you gave me, I got all right before reaching the
Bluff.
I really must congratulate you on having
dropped on such a good thing. Its effect on me
was wonderful, and I doctored several people on
the steamer, who were suffering with influenza,
with the Marupa, and in each case I put them
right in no time.
I am sorry to say that I have not a drop left,
but shall get you to give me a supply when I
return to New Zealand : and I never intend doing
without it in future. When my throat has been
bad, and I have had to sing, I took Marupa, and
consider it a splendid thing for anyone with a
voice.
I wish you good luck with it, but the thing is
bound to sell well.
Yours very truly,
A. J. Chamberlin.
Lyell, May 27, 1892,
Messrs. Kempthorne, Prosser and Co.,
Wellington.
Gentlemen, —I am pleased to inform you that I
nave sold all the Marupa you sent me, and more
pleased to tell you that not in one single instance
has it failed to give relief, or, to put it in the
words of a highly respectable lady of this town,
whose husband had not had one hour’s sleep for
several nights, notwithstanding several prescrip-
tions from doctors, which gave no relief. The
Irst teaspoonful of Marupa he took acted like
magic, giving immediate relief, and he went to
sleep, and had a comfortable night’s rest, a thing
unknown to him for the last month.
Please send six dozen Marupa at first oppor-
tunity, and oblige.
Yours faithfully,
J. Fennell.
A TRIP UP THE WANGANUI RIVER,
From the Otago Witness.
When we reached Hruharama (Jerusalem) we
went ashore to pay our respects to Sister Mary
Joseph, whose name and fame have recently been
blazoned forth in the chemists’ shops and advertis-
ing columns of the newspapers of the Colony.
We found the old lady at home dispensing
medicinal aid to a number of waiting Natives
in front of the mission buildings, where there
is a level grassy space overshadowed by well-
grown oaks of no inconsiderable size. We
found her a pleasant, shrewd, old French-
woman, speaking English with a slight
foreign accent, and she received us graciously.
Asking us to excuse her for a few minutes, she
attended to the wants of the waiting Maoris, and
then invited us to inspect the schoolroom. She
informed us that it was a difficult matter to do
much with the Maori children, as they are sub-
jected to little, if any, parental control, being free
to go to school or run wild as they list, and they
choose the latter alternative —as I fear most child-
ren, whether white or dusky, would if left to their
own sweet will. The good sisters, of whom there
are eight, have some half-dozen white children of
vicious parents as the nucleus of their school, and
we saw these children at play in the sunshine,
looking healthy and happy. The old lady in-
formed us that progress was rather slow, but that
they had influenced the Maoris for good. A neat
little church, with a lofty, tapering spire, stands
on an elevation above the village, forming a
pleasing feature in the landscape, and is a monu-
ment to the self-denying labours of the sisterhood
by means of which the funds have been recently
provided for its erection, the former church having
been burnt down not long ago. Besides the nuns
there is a missionary priest resident here, who
occasionally visits Ranana, where there is also a
little church. The rev. father has also another
flock besides his dusky parishioners, as he owns
700 acres of land, which he has acquired from the
natives, and which has been cleared of bush and
sown down in English grass. Sister Mary
hospitably presented us_ with some enormous
and delicious apples, besides a glass of raspberry
vinegar and water.
CURIOUS FACTS
ABOUT FORSES
JIBBING NOT A VICE.
Mayhew, the well-known authority on horses,
gays: “Jibbing is not a vice, but a nervous dis-
order—a sort of equine epilepsy. A word spoken
sharply can summon the attack, which generally
deprives the animal of all power of motion, or
forces it to move in a contrary direction to that
desired. The movements are independent of the
will; and if any person will attentively examine
the countenance of a horse when in the act of
jibbing, the real character of the supposed vice
will be recognised. When jibbing is exhibited, a
spasmodic fit has possession of the frame. It is
useless to flog or inflict other tortures. The attack
will last a certain time, and then, perhaps,
suddenly vanish. No brutality can shorten its
duration, though cruelty may possibly lengthen
the convulsion. The horse should, in such a case,
be allowed to stand quietly, the harness loosened
where tight, and the bridle held to prevent him
bolting, as often happens when a horse regains his
senses after a fit. This form of epilepsy is a
distinct malady, from which a horse seldom
recovers, and it would be an act of humanity to
put the sufferer out of his misery at once.”
What a large percentage of people own horses
and know little or nothing of the nature and ail-
ments of the animal! In the interest of this
patient, willing, dumb servant we think that
veterinary science might well be included in the
list of technical subjects taught in our schools.
Owners of horses should at least be well versed in
the simple ailments of a horse, and know what
remedies to apply. In the meantime, for the
benefit of those unacquainted with the fact, we
may say that for sprains, swollen legs, and the
like, St. Jacobs Oil is a sure and safe remedy,
acting as a certain pain conqueror and healer in
the case of the beast, as it is in that of the human
being. Thousands of proofs have been adduced
of this important fact, of which the following is
one : Mr. Thomas Wade, of 4 Hunt-street, Surry
Hills, writes : I have used St. .Jacobs Oil for ray
horse with capital results. He had one of his
forelegs swelled to a great size, and was very lame.
I was advised to try St. Jacobs Oil for it and did
so. I bought two bottles, but one was sufficient;
the first application brought down the swelling,
and the leg was completely cured in a very short
time.

A Melbourne magistrate has decided that
if a bar-door is locked and the key left in it the
door is not locked.

The Perth (W.A.) Daily News says:
“Mr. J. P. T. Caulfield, the Boy-Politician, a
fluent and able speaker, with an alert mind, and
considerable political knowledge, will most likely
make his home in Perth, where he is now staying.
We want men such as he in our midst, and many
of them.” The trouble of it is that there aren’t
many of them—in fact, there is only one and there
may never be another.
Dear Bulletin, —ln 1861, on the Hunter,
“Black Peter” threw a boomerang. Twenty
years elapsed, and Peter migrated to Queensland,
then to a wanner sphere. In January, 1890, the
boomerang faithfully returned to its point of de-
parture. It exhibited unmistakable signs of grief
at Peter’s want of confidence, and then disap-
peared due north. A Queensland man has since
told the writer that it found Peter’s grave, in-
scribed his epitaph on a tree, and then left to look
up his soul. But Queensland men are mostly liars.
Yours, J. S. S.
On the unveiling of a statue of Hercules in
Victoria Square, Adelaide, Horn, M.P., tried to
sneer down the miners and their “lock-out”
struggle by stating that “Hercules was at one
time a groom in the Augean stables, and finding
the work was greater than he bargained for he
promptly struck.” That, Hercules was entitled
to do, but, as a matter of fabled fact, he didn’t,
and Rogers, of the T. and L. Council, wrote to an
Adelaide paper showing how Hercules, like the
Broken Hill miners, fulfilled his share of the con-
tract, while Augeas, like the mine-directors,
refused the reward to which he (Hercules) was
justly entitled, and so provoked the struggle in
which that king and unjust employer lost his life.
Horn’s analogy was unfortunate.
The culture of beauty has been a recog-
nised art in all ages and all countries and flourishes
to-day as it has in the past. Quite the most
successful specialist of modern times is Mrs. Anna
Ruppert, an American lady doctor, whose fame as
a beautifier extends over two hemispheres, and
whose ability has placed her in an altogether
unique position. Many Australian ladies have
visited Mrs. Ruppert in London and have seen
her vast reception and consulting rooms, offices
and laboratory, but for those who cannot consult
her personally she has recently opened a branch
office in Australia, at 153 Strand, Sydney,
further particulars concerning which may be
gleaned from advertisement in this paper.
Some fine writing from a Melbourne
evening paper. The reporter has been watching
shearers: —
The pure white inner side of the rich fleeces were drop-
ping to the dirty floor like a snowy cascade, revealing the
pink skin beneath the “swansdown” that was left like
the dainty blush of a bride behind her veil.


“August
Flower”
Mrs. Sarah M. Black of Seneca,
Mo., during the past two years has
been affected with Neuralgia of the
Head, Stomach and Womb, and
writes: ‘‘ My food did not seem to
strengthen me at all and my appe-
tite was very variable. My face
was yellow, my head dull, and I had
such pains in my left side. In the
morning when I got up I would
have a flow of mucus in the mouth,
and a bad, bitter taste. Sometimes
my breath became short, and I had
such queer, tumbling, palpitating
sensations around the heart. I ached
all day under the shoulder blades,
in the left side, and down the back
of my limbs. It seemed to be worse
in the wet, cold weather of Winter
and Spring; and whenever the spells
came on, my feet and hands would
turn cold, and I could get no sleep
at all. I tried everywhere, and got
no relief before using August Flower
Then the change came. It has done
me a wonderful deal of good during
the time I have taken it and is work-
ing a complete cure. ” ®
G. G. GREEN, Sole Man’fr, Woodbury, N.J.

“ Young Peel,” writes The Bulletin's correspondent,
“was very much en evidence at the premiere of HadJou-
Chambers’ ‘Queen of Manoa,’ and made himself quite con-
spicuous by the care lie took to see that all doors were
closed through which draughts might assail the Langtry.
Abington Baird seems quite ‘out Of it.’ ”
Nothing more disgraceful has been seen in Sydney
journalism for a long time, than the wholesale crib by a
certain critic (in his notice of “Genevievede Brabant”)
of the serpentine-dance description.
Pollard’s juveniles do several little things in “The
Gondoliers” better than the adults did them, but the
best points of Gilbertian dialogue are not to be picked up,
parrot-fashion, by girls who have the vaguest notion as to
where the joke comes in.
The beauteous Robert Inman got married recently at
the Thames (M.L.) to Miss Maud Appleton, the leading
lady of his co. The wedding was a good “ ad.,” and busi-
ness for the rest of the season was so big that Inman
thinks of getting married again whenever things are a
little slack.
London Gaiety Co. traces its poor biz. to the death of the
Duke of Clarence, who, they say, “ Patronised this theatre
more than any other.” What an epitaph !
They say that a tenor voice is a disease. If so, what
a lot of unemployed diseases there are about Melbourne,
just now !
“A daughter of the beautiful Alice Dunning Lingard
has just married a son of the lion comique, Charles
Godfrey.” These are the things that make one feel old.
Janet Achurch is engaged by Beerbohm Tree, in
London. A critic pertinently says Janet has every gift,
except the knowledge how to use them.
Nellie Stewart is not returning to Australia yet awhile.
Musician Turner, of the Melbourne Exhibition Pops.,
strokes his beard, and says plaintively: “You know I
only get what’s left.” Yet Turner has made a lot more
money this year than even the theatrical managerial
panjandrum.
Mrs. Arthur Leslie, a daughter (Nina) of P. P. Laber-
touche, of Melbourne, is making quite a name for herself,
in London, as the writer of pretty song-words.
Mrs. Langtry says; “A woman of the deadest white
skin, with blue eyes and blonde hair, becomes a poem
when she dons a yellow gown.” Whereupon a New York
dramatic journal thus comments, “ and the man who pays
for it becomes a tragedy in three acts when he sees the
bill,"
Patti gets £1000 a night, 40 nights, in America, next
year.
Bandmann, playing lago, once finished the famous
speech with, “To suckle beer and chronicle small females.”
What a fate it would be !
William Morton, of Greenwich, claims to be proprietor
of the only temperance theatre in England. Jules
Joubert had the honour of building the first temperance
theatre (the Haleck) in Melbourne! but he never assumed
any frill over the matter. In point of fact, poor Jules
claimed a liquor-license, but couldn’t obtain one for want
of back-door influence to work the oracle.
The little actress always carries her purse in her hand.
What does she carry to the theatre in her little reticule?
Her costume, says somebody, but it is the bit of jewellery
she has scraped up. Was an actress ever carried out of a
theatre in a violoncello-case, when creditors were on hei
track ? ...
The “Faust” and “Carmen” Gaiety burlesques have
been produced at Berlin and Vienna respect!' ely.
Gorgeous Grace Huntley is the Escamillo engaged to
“knock” the Germans.
At Ramsgate (Eng.), the other day, a roaring lion
escaped from its cage in Sanger’s Circus, and caused
brave men to walk over fair women in their anxiety to
quit the show. There was no cause for alarm, however.
The monarch of the woods simply bolted after its trainer
and began licking his boots.
Florence St. John is playing “Blue-eyed Susan,” vice
Nellie Stewart, now that the comic opera has gone “on
tour,” This arrangement seems to say more for the opera
itself than for the success of the original leading lady.
The authors and composer have, it appears, worked tip
the name part for Marius’s wife.
F. C. Brewer chatters about old convict theatricals in
N.S. Wales, and tells how, frequently', real trees, grass,
and water were utilised for scenery ; real blood, where
this fluid was an accessory ; and even the banquet was no
sham of tinselled goblets and painted viands, but a sub-
stantial “feed” of “damper,” beef, and mutton, washed
down with plentiful potations of “ Royal George tea.”
This fluid was made in a three-legged pot, the lea, sugar
and milk being all put in together and boiled.
The Brothers Gatti have taken over the balance of lease
of the London Vaudeville from Thomas Thorne. The
price fixed for the lease is something short of £16,000,
so Thorne, who is getting towards old age, won’t retire
from management with very much in hand when all his
accounts are squared up. He was a rich man when “ Our
Boys” had completed its three years’ run.
The story that Minnie Palmer had been engaged to play
Farren’spart in a revised edition of “ Cinder Ellen,” at
the Gaiety, was all moonshine.
In the Contemporary, George Barlow eulogises “the
rising star of the genius of Darraont.” Some of “ the
rough-and-tumble critics” of Australia recognised Dar-
mont’s power and advised Bernhardt to push him more
to the front. Contracts with other actors, however, kept
him out of “ Camille,” “ La Tosca,” and other plays that
would have suited him while here. In London he seems
to have been the sole lead.
L. L. Smith takes a prodigious interest in the Aquarium
at Melbourne Exhibition, and has dowered it with
pictures, of a rather w'arm order. There is a fine casket,
given by Louis XVI. to Marie Antoinette, and also by
Louis L. Smith to Mrs. L. L. A melancholy interest
attaches to several of Haydon’s paintings here.
What idiots they were to kill the cow that attacked
Gladstone! She would have been worth her weight in
gold to a circus. However, her calf goes to Chicago Fair.
G. W. Appleton, Brigand Stanley’s former agent, says
our London correspondent, is at law with the great
African nigger-slayer for commission in connection with
certain lecturing tours. It was Appleton who brought
Stanley and the Much-Travelled together. Stanley paid
com. on that job, but it appears he put a creditor of
Appleton’s on to garnishee this sum, hence late litigation
and a good deal of bad feeling. Appleton promises us a
treat if he should ever succeed in getting the Brigand
into the witness-box. May I be there to hear 1 Stanley
is lying very low just now. His abortive electoral cam-
paign in Lambeth has convinced even the faithful
Dorothy of the man’s utter shallowness when he gets
away from the one subject which has made his name.
Dear Bulletin,— Re Pattie Brown’s alteration of name.
We used to call her “ Sallie,” but the young lady informed
me in Melbourne that Emilie Melville had altered the
name to “ Pattie ” when the cast was made out for the
“ Grand Duchess,” in which the one-time Sallie played
Prince Paul.— Prompt Corner.
Sara Bernhardt complains that she could not act in
Australia, because of the audience keeping their eyes on
the books, and the rustle of turning the leaves, as in
church according to Sara’s reminiscences of some time
in the last century. One man reading a newspaper in a
theatre may spoil a whole “ Hamlet.”
A London dramatist having claimed the authorship of
an adaptation from a French play, a critic writes that he
is no more the author than a monthly nurse is the
mother.
Vi. Varley is to marry “Dick” Hayhow, of the office
of Howard Smith & Sons, shipowners.
What manager for the Melbourne Bijou ? Garner?
It is odd to see the crowds thronging through the
vestibule bars to the nightly evangelistic services at the
Melbourne Royal.
As emphasising the remarks made in an
article “Theology in State Schools” (printed
elsewhere in to-day’s Bulletin) we quote Bishop
Thornton, of Victoria, who lately made some
remarks about “ replacing the Christian Scriptures
in the national education of this Protestant Chris-
tian colon///'
Herr Rassmussen, the well-known Danish
herbalist of Sydney and Melbourne, lias received
from the Academic des Inventeurs Industrie!s of
Paris a ponderous gold medal, a beautiful work
of art, in recognition of his services in discovering
a new and greatly improved process for extract-
ing medicinal properties from plants.

What He Did With It.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Multon’s pub. at Bilboola. Half-a-dozen bush-
men in the bar, drinking and arguing, and behind
the counter Joe Multon himself, with care upon
his corrugated brow, and one fat hand absently
feeling,for his long-departed hair.
“ What’s the matter with you, Joe?”
“Never you mind, Billy Hancock. It’s nothing
you can remedy, anyhow; so just, drink your
liquor and mind your own Hallo! Sandy.
How are you, my boy ? Very man I wanted to
see! That is, if you’re bound for Griffithstown
to-day.”
“ Ay, ay, man ! That’s where I’m gaun,” said
the new-comer —a long, slab-sided Scotchman,
with a huge red beard, and little, waggish grey
eyes, not entirely innocent of squint. “So, gie’s
a drap o’ whusky, an’ if ye want me for onything,
cut it as short’s ye can, for I’m no gaun to sit
doon amang sic an ungodly crew as ye hao gotten
aboot ye the-day !”
A roar of laughter followed this announcement,
for Sandy Macpherson was a general favourite,
and every man there knew that, if whisky were in
question, Sandy would sit do\Vn with the Devil
and all his angels.
“Come into the back-room a minute,” said
Multon, when Sandy had had his nobbier,
and Macpherson followed him in. “ This is what
I want you to do for me : There’s an acceptance of
mine coming up from Sydney —due at the bank at
Griffithstown to-morrow, it is—and I want to
lodge the money to meet it. Will you take it
down for me, like a good fellow? I can’t leave
the place, myself, at present, and there’s nobody
about here I’d like to send. Will you do it ?”
“Surely, man—surely ! —if ye like to trust me
wi’ the siller.”
“ With ten times the amount, old man ! Only,
for God’s sake, don’t get on the spree, going
down. The hard-stuff plays up with you some-
times, you know !”
“Dinna fash yoursel’ aboot that, man! I’ll
joost wat ma whustle again, before I stairt, an’
deil anither drap gaes doon it till your money’s
safe in the bank.”
But, alas for Sandy’s resolve ! Before his old
mare had carried him half-a-dozen miles upon his
journey, it began to rain—one of those persistent
Scotch drizzles which Sandy was unpatriotic
enough to detest with all his soul. In half-an-
hour he was as wet as a platypus, and thus it was
that,' when, at a turn of the track, he came in
sight of Kelly’s, he pulled up to “conseeder the
maitter,” as he called it.
“Ye wasna to tak ony mair whusky till ye’d
pit the money in the bank,” he said to himself, as
he wistfully gazed at the pub. “ But then, ye see,
Sandy, ma man, ye didna expec’ sic a damnable
drookin’. It wasna in the bond, ye ken, sae I’ll
joost let ye hae a neep or twa at Paddy Kelly’s,
tae keep the cauld oot o’ your auld caircase; an’
then, aff ye go again, douce an’ sober !”
But, in Kelly’s bar, as it happened, there was
at that moment —drinking hell-fire rum and
cursing the squatter and all his works —a brother
Caledonian, of whom and Sandy it might truly
have been said, “they had been fou’ for weeks
thegither.” The nip or two, therefore, became in
time the nip or twenty, so that, when Sandy
betook himself once more to his saddle, it was
only his mare’s previous experience of such
episodes that enabled him to stay in it. The
magician in his stomach, however, had not yet
attained full command of his seasoned brain. He
pushed steadily on for Griffithstown, nor was it
till an hour had been occupied in getting over
about four or five miles that he once more pulled
up to “conseeder.”
“ It appears to me, Sandy Macphairson,” he
said, as with no small difficulty he engineered
himself on to the ground—“ it appears to me that,
if ye’re no drunk, ye’re in a state vara unpleasantly
resemblin’ that condeetion. Sae, aboot the best
thing ye can do, I’m theenkin’, is to lay yersel’
doon an’ hae a quiet snooze ! ”
The moon was bright, and a slip-panel was close
at hand, so within a couple of minutes Sandy and
his steed were trespassers —Sandy oblivious to
that and every other consideration, and the mare
crop-cropping round him in the silence of the
night.
As the hours went by, however, the air grew
chilly, and Sandy, who scorned the effete refine-
ment of a waistcoat, began to stir and mutter, and
finally sat up and rubbed his eyes. “Whaur the
deil !” he was beginning, when the sight of his
mare supplied a clue to what had happened. To
part of it, at least, for, though he well enough
remembered his arrival at Kelly’s, his departure
from that distinguished hostelry was a sealed book
to him.
“Eh, Sandy, mon,” he soliloquised, as he
stumbled to his feet, “ye maun hae had an awfu’
dose ! Your heid’s like a turnip, an’ your thrapple
like a limekiln ! If God’s ever gaun to gie ye a
bit sense, it’s amaist time He was theenkin’ o’t.
Ye enjoy the disteenetion, at this present moment,
o’ bein’ ane of the biggest fules in Australia ! Ye
hadna even the gumption to fetch a big flaisk wi’
ye,” he went on, feeling himself all over. “ Eh,
stay, though ! —what’s this?”
“This ” was a hard lump on his chest. There
was something bulky in the inside pocket of his
Crimean shirt. Out it came in a jiffy—a little
canvas bag, at which he gazed in boozy astonish-
ment. Then there was a faint chink, as he shook
it, and in a flash lie remembered —Multon’s money,
in notes, gold and silver.
Sandy fairly gasped as he looked around him.
“ An’ it’s here ye were lyin’, Sandy Macphairson !”
he exclaimed. “ Deid drunk, in full sicht o’ the
road, wi’ a hunnerd an’ saxty poon at the sairvice
o’ ony rogue that happened to come alang an’ see
ye ! I telt ye, the noo, that ye were ane o’ the
biggest fules in the colony. Ye’re mair, man—
ye’re mair !—ye’re joost the biggest eediot in the
hale o’ the airth ! Gome here, Jess !” he cried,
snatching at his mare’s bridle, and clambering
hastily into the saddle. “We’re too lang here,
baith you an’ mysel’! Let’s see—l ken this place
weel enough ; we’re no vara far frae Griffithstown.
Aff ye go, then, as if ye were Tam O’Shanter’s
Meg, wi’ a’ the warlocks ahint her ! ”
The sun was getting low, on the following day,
when Sandy pulled up once more at Multon’s
door.
“Well, Sandy,” said Multon, as Macpherson
came slowly into the bar. “ Back again ? Banked
the coin all right, I suppose? Why, what’s the
matter, man? You look as if you’d seen a
ghost ! ”
“Maitter enough, Mr. Multon,” said poor
Sandy. “Your money—l may as weel oot wi’ it
at once —your money’s gone ! ”
The publican stared at him for a moment; then
he walked round outside the counter. “ Here ! ”
he said, “what cursed rubbish is this you’re
talking, Mac ? The money gone ! Are you mad,
or drunk ?”
“ It’s the plain truth I’m tellin’ ye, Multon. I
got drunk yesterday at Kelly’s, an’ I dinna ken
what’s become o’ the siller !’
Multon took him by the arm and walked him
into the back-room. “Now, look here, Macpher-
son,” he said, “ let’s have no more of this ! I’ve
known you a good few years, and I’d be sorry to see
you get into trouble. Turn up the stuff, and not
a soul shall know anything of this but our two
selves ! ”
Sandy groaned. “Will ye no’ understand,
man ? It’s the truth I’m tellin’ ye. I got full up
to the neck at Paddy Kelly’s, an’ what’s come o’
the siller I ken nae mair than the deid ! Yo
dinna surely theenk I wad tak’ it mysel’? Ye
said ye’d trust me wi’ ten times as muckle !”
“ Ay,” returned Multon, gloomily, “and an
infernal fool I was to think it! But I’m wasting
time. Do you refuse to say what you’ve done
with my money ?”
“I can say nae mair nor what I’ve said already,
Mr. Multon.”
“ We’ll see ! Ned !”—calling a boy in from the
yard—“ go across to the police-earnp and ask
Coleman to step over here. I’m sorry you drive
me to this, Macpherson, but your yarn’s too thin
—altogether too thin for an old hand like me, and
a hundred and sixty pounds ain’t got together in
a hurry these times.”
Coleman was Bilboola’s solitary trooper.
“ What’s up, Multon ?” he said as ho canie in.
“Somebody been kickin’ up a shine ?” “ Shines ”
of a more or less drunken order made nearly all
the business that fell to his lot in the sleepy little
township, and very unprofessionally sorry he
looked when Multon explained what the matter
was.
“I believe he’s telling the truth,” said the con-
stable when Sandy had described, as far as his
memory extended, his adventures of the previous
day. “Paddy Kelly’s is getting to be a precious
queer shop. It wants looking after. And you
don’t remember leaving there? You must have
been pretty well tanked !”
“ Deil a thing I mind frae the time I was sittin’
in Kelly’s bar till one o’ your men—Gray it was—
woke me up on Maxwell’s verandah this mornin’.”
“Where was your horse?”
“Stannin’ close by. That was what Gray
spoke aboot. He couldna hae me lyin’ aboot the
street like that, wi’ ma beast loose, an’ if I didna
clear oot o’ Griffithstown quick he’d lumber me !”
“ Ay ! he’s always in a mighty hurry with his
lumbering, that young man. He’ll put his foot in
it one of these days. Well, Multon, what d’ye
want me for?”
“ Want you for?” repeated the publican. “Why,
to do your duty, of course !”
“ Just so. But my duty doesn’t call upon me
to take this man in charge—of my own accord,
that is. In fact, I believe he’s been robbed, him-
self.”
“Never mind what you believe.- Do you refuse
to arrest him ?”
“ Do you give him in charge?—that’s the point!”
Multon hesitated for half a minute. “Yes,”
he said then. “ I believe he’s planted the stuff.
He’s a devilish sight too wide-awake to have a
bag of money like that taken from him without
his knowledge.”
“No man’s wide-awake when he’s asleep,” re-
turned the trooper, with which incontrovertible
proposition he laid his official hand on Mac-
pherson’s "shoulder. “I must take you to the
station, Sandy,” he said. “And you, Mr. Multon,
must come over and have the charge entered.”
“Now, Sandy,” said Coleman, when they had
got to the camp, “ I’m sorry, but I must search
you, my lad.”
“Ye maun do your duty, I suppose,” said
Sandy, as he handed over his ancient sombrero to
have its lining examined. Next came his pockets;
knife, pipe, tobacco, matches, one-and-ninepence
—nothing more. Nothing inside the shirt;
nothing in the trousers,
“Now take off your boots !” and off came
Sandy’s well-patched Wellingtons. The trooper
thrust his hand into the right boot, threw it down
and picked up the other. “Hallo!” he exclaimed,
as he drew out a folded paper. “ What have we
got here?” Then he looked at the inside, and
gave a long whistle.
“ What is it ?” said Multon, impatiently. “ It’s
not money, at all events !”
“ Perhaps it’s as good as money, though,” re-
turned Coleman. “Do you remember anything
about going to the bank last night, Sandy ? ”
A light sprang into Sandy’s lugubrious visage.
“ Guid God ! man—yes ! ” he cried. “Noo that
ye speak o’t, I hae a sort o’ recklection o’ seein’
Wilkinson, the manager.”
“ And you don’t remember what passed, eh?”
“ Deil a bit ! It’s like a dream to me that I
saw his face somewhere. Ye surely don’t mean”—
“Bank of New South Wales, Griffithstown,”
interrupted the trooper, reading from the paper
he had found. “ 10th Sept., 1890. Received from
Mr. Alexander Macpherspn, on account of Mr.
Joseph Multon, Bilboola, the sum of one hundred
and sixty pounds sterling. F. G. Wilkinson,
manager.”
“ Oh, Lord ! ” said Multon, with a gasp of
relief. “ I hope it’s true ! ”
“True?” echoed Coleman, handing him the
paper. “You know Wilkinson’s signature, I
suppose ?”
“ Yes, yes! this is his writing, sure enough.
But this isn’t the usual form of bank-receipt.
There’s something crooked about the affair, still,
I’m afraid. Macpherson couldn't have gone to the
bank without knowing it —I can’t believe it! ”
“ It’s easy to settle the point. Telegraph to
Wilkinson. You ought to get an answer in ten
minutes, or so. Sandy’ll stay here in the mean-
time.”
Off rushed Multon to the telegraph-office on the
other side of the way.
“Any money lodged to my credit yesterday?
Please reply at once.”
Then he waited at the counter; in a quarter of
an hour came the answer.
“Yes. Hundred and sixty. Macpherson.
Knocked me up at midnight. Drunk, afraid
losing it. Gave provisional receipt. Bill retired.
F. G. Wilkinson.”
That settled the business. Sandy—though to
this day he cannot recall the circumstances—had
not only lodged the money, but had, with drunken
caution, stowed the receipt away in his boot,
Multon, they say, gave him a ten-pound-note, but
swore, nevertheless, that from that time forward
he would make all his payments personally.

Whose Wife?
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“Harry ! what that yerself, back toold Vic., man,
Down from the Never Land? Now, what’s yer
game ?
Ugly as ever. Not dropped the old trick, man?
Well, what’ll yer take with me? Give it a
name.
“Here long? Well, rather, lad; five years and
over,
Settled for good, an’ supportin’ a wife.
Slipped from the saddle, an’ livin’ in clover,
Swore off a whole heap, an’ I’ve slung the old
life.
“ What’s come of Taffy, an’ Brum, an’ the rest of
’em ?
How long since you broke with the Poverty
push ? ”
“ Well, Bill, you’re on top, you’ve the best of the
best of ’em.
Poor Brum is a dummy, Taff died in the bush ;
“ Bob’s cookin’ for Chows on an absentee’s station,
Sara’s toutin’ for spielers, Pete’s lumbered for
life ;
I’m married an’ trampin’ half over creation,
Trackin’ a woman, my runaway wife.
“ Left me six years ago—sloped ! I was shearin’
Away on the Thomson. She skipped ’thout a
word.
Last year she was seen by a Barcoo man, steerin’
Round about here, au’ that’s all that I’ve heard.
“ Heard of her, know her, Bill—tallish and
clever,
Blue eyes, an’ dark hair, an’ she’s branded
here, so.
Not the woman to liquor, or go on the never,
But skittish an’ queer in her tantrums, yer
know.
“ This is her picture, Bill; just have a look at her.
That like any female you chance to have seen ?
Halloo! here, hold up ! Say, man, what’s the
matter ?
Your wife ! By the Lord, Morton, what do
you mean ?”
EDWARD DYSON.

The Age is very mad at Fitchett for

The expression, “Go, and put your head
in a bag” has acquired a ghastly significance.
At Sunbury (Vic.) some young fellows put a bag
over the head of a drunken man, and he died
immediately.
Characteristic utterance of Renan: “I
often think that the judgments to be passed upon
each of us in the Valley of Jehosaphat will be none
other than the judgments of women countersigned
by the Eternal.”
From the S.M. Herald :
Few poets have poured such copious streams into “ the
well of English undefiled” which is the glory of England,
and in those streams there is not one atom of filth. That
cannot be said of Shakespeare.
The quotation refers to Tennyson? No, to
Chaucer. Chaucer! and doesn’t mean but
read “The Miller’s Tale.”
Dear Bulletin, —The Caucasian subji*
gates the negro or Kanaka. The Mongol goes one
better. Not long ago in passing a Chinese garden
(within two miles of the centre of one of our large
cities) I observed a heathen engaged in ploughing.
That fact was not remarkable excepting that the
Chinee seldom uses a plough, preferring rather
the more conservative method of digging. But,
instead of a horse, a white woman was hitched to
the plough, and pulled with a will and energy
worthy of a much higher form of labour. Thus
the Mongol subjugates the Caucasian ! Who will
subjugate the MongoI?— JOSEPHUS.
Private letters from Roeburn, W.A., give
a cheerful account of that breezy locality. Roe-
burn is remarkable for salt-bush, wind, and
settled melancholy. All houses in the locality
have their roofs chained to the ground, because
when a wind-storm comes along it shifts every-
thing that has been left out, and uproots the
post-holes. On a recent occasion the breezes
removed the sheet-iron off one man’s roof, and
folded it so neatly around the verandah-posts of a
neighbour that the latter was prosecuted for
stealing the iron, and the bench was asked to
believe he’d fastened it about his verandah-posts
to keep the cats from climbing on to his roof to
fight o’ nights.
Here is what Gibbon, the historian, ha s
to say about that holy man George (of Saint®
Michael and George)
“ George, from his parents or his education surnamed
the Cappadocian, was born at Epiphania, in Cilicia, in a
fuller’s shop. From this obscure and servile origin he
raised himself by the talents of a parasite; and the
patrons whom he assiduously flattered procured for
their worthless dependent a lucrative commission or con-
tract to supply the army with bacon. He accumulated
wealth by the basest arts of fraud and corruption ; but
his malversations were so notorious that George was com-
pelled to escape from the pursuit of justice. After this
disgrace, in which he appears to have saved his fortune at
the expense of his honour, he embraced with real or
affected zeal the profession of Arianism.” And so on.
